The Philippines is the first of a series of countries to be\nassessed. The primary purpose of this Philippines study is to identify\napplications with high P/V Males potential so that P/V equipment suppliers\nand distributors could develop appropriate marketing strategies. The\nfollowing essential information for the Philippines was gathered.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Estimates of potential market size for P/V power applications in\nthe agricultural sector.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Power requirements and usage profiles for a wide variety of agricultural applications which are compatible with a P/V system.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Operating and cost characteristics of gasoline and diesel power\nsystems that would compete against P/V systems.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Energy, agriculture and development goals. programs and policies\nwhich will influence P/V sales.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Appropriate financing mechanisms and estimates of capital available\nfor P/V system purchases.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Channels for di.tribution. installation and maintenance of P/V\nsystems.\n\t\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Appropriate methods for conducting business in the Philippines.\nThe types of applications considered are those requiring less than 15KW\nof power and operating in a stand-alone configuration without back-up power.\nThese applications include irrigation. post-harvest operations. food and\nfiber processing and storage, and livestock and fisheries operations.\nStudy Approach\nA team from DHR. Incorporated and Associates in Rural Development. Inc.\nconducted a month long study in the Philippines during October and November\n\nx\n\nI\n\n1980. The major activities of the team were a arias of meetings with Pilipino\nenergy, economic, financial. business and policy experts to obtain current\ndata and their evaluation of factors important to introducing P/V power systems\ninto the agriculture sector. Site visits were made to obtain power requirements\nand energy use profile data for several agricultural applications. In addition\nto data collection o the team members gave presentations on P/V systems. and\n\ndistributed sets of brochures consisting of advertising material obtained from\nU.S. P/V companies and from U.S. government sources.\nThe information gathered served so a data base to characterise the onvironwent in which P/V systems would be marketed and used. Zar s on applications was\nused to identify cost-competitive and-uses. The potential market alas estimate\nwas based on cost-competitiveness, availability of finance, business environment, and other factors. Due to the inherent uncertainties present in such\nanslysss, throe - otsntial nwarket estimates are presented - "Status quo." "increased activity." and \'Optimistic."\nStatus of P/V in Philippines Energy Development Plans\nPhilippines is highly dependent on foreign oil for co me rclal energy.\nIn 1980, oil accounted for about 90 percent of the annual commercial energy\nconsumption, or more than double the level of 15 years ago. The rising oil\nbill has made the Philippines extremely aware of the ijW : rtance of changing\nIts energy source six and improving consumption efficiency. Domestic oil,\ngeothermal. coal. hydroelectric and a number of other renewable and nonconventional energy sources are viewed as major possibilities for helping\nreduce oil imports. The Philippines have embarked on an ambitious five year\nprogram aimed at reducing their dependence on foreign oil. Strategy components\nsupporting this plan include:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 The accelerated diversification from depletables to alternative\nsources of energy. with *Mhasts on indigenously abundant and\n\nregenerative forms.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Tne establishment of support infrastructure to enable efficient\nprocessing. handling, storage, distribution and marketing of\ntraditional and new energy resources.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Application of appropriate decentralised energy facilities for arwas\ndifficult and costly to connect to centralised systems.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 The strengthening of collaborative efforts with other nations in\nemergency supply sharing and technical expertise interchange.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 The concentration of energy research and development on energy resources\nthat are locally abundant for dispersed applications.\nThese strategies would favorably influence the use of P/V in the\nSowever, the toverasent has ^;o t included a sisxnificart\n\nPhilippines.\n\nus to the abeam; of government s torts to apply famlIt" and cost-effective\ntechnologies, and to wait for the more industrialised and developed countries\nto develop and reduce the costs of more experimental and sower technologies.\nP/V systems are clearly viewed as an sxperimental technology in the official\ngovernment energy plans.\n\nThe Philippines has embarked on an ambitious rural electrification program\nwhich expects to fully electrify the country before the and of the decade. While\nthis would dampen the deN4nd for P/V use agriculture, DHR does not expect it to\nhave too great an effect on the market. This is due to the fact that many agricultural applications would not be served by the "backbone" type rural electrification system being promoted irk the Philippines.\nimplications of Philippines Agricultural Development Plans for P/V Systems\nThere are several important development policies :--d programs within the\ngovernment agriculture sector that relate specifically r. opportunities for\nP/V systems. The interest in P/V systems for use in agricultural products\nbeing promoted by the government will be greater than in those products with a\nlesser rota in the development plans. The major crops in which increased production\nIs being strongly supported are rice and corn. Secondarily, programs in cattle,\ndairy, and cotton are considered of critical importance, although production\nhas not been increasing as fast as intended. The crops discussed below are chosen\ndue to their importance for P/V applications throughout all production phases of\nthe crop.\nR\n\nRICE--Though the Philippines has reached self-sufficiency in rice,\na 4.4 r4rcent annual increase in rice production has been targeted\nfor tte 4 978-1982 period to keep up wirh population growth an.. provide\nan adequ.:e buffer stock. Of more importance to P/V applications\nis the attempt by the government and farmers to reduce use of fossil\nfuels and nonrenewable energy in rice production and processing.\nImproved irrigation and methods of water pumping are a continuing\npriority. However, there have been increased crop losses recently\ndue to nonuse of powered irrigation, as farmers have not wanted to\npay the increased cost of gasoline. P/V sya.ems would also be\nimportant in rice drying and milling.\n\ne CARD --Increasing corn production for buman consumption is a major\nemphasis at this time due to a large domestic production deficit,\nand the resulting importation of about 120,000 metric tons per year.\nMoreover, the key limiting factor to increasing animal production\nin the Philippines is the lack of feed grains. Production is\ntargeted to increase by 20 to 25 percent over the next six years.\nThis increased production is meant to reduce the need for imported\ncorn and to increase the availability of corn as an animal feed.\nA role for P/V power is expected in irrigation, drying, and grinding.\na OTHER CROPS--There are several crops for which, although P/V does\nnot sees a likely power source in actual production $ it could be\nutilised in support facilities and at central building complexes\non large farms. Sugarcane. bananas, pineapple, and casava are crops\nin this category. However. a role for P/V power systems for irrigation of high value crops such as spices, garlic, etc., is most\nfeasible.\ne 7151 PROD=ION--Agusculture\nare a priority area in the\nrole for P/V systems is in\nwater Pumping and aeration\n\nand cos3mercial fisheries developrct\ngovernment development plans. A major\nlc* making for fish preservation and for\nin aquaculture.\n\nxii\ni\n\nAvailability of Financing Mechanisms and Funds\nThe Philippines has an extensive financial network composed of both private and government-owned financial institutions. The majority of investment\nfunds are controlled by the privately owned, commercial and savings bank sector.\nThese funds are available as short-term working capital loans primarily invested in industry, consumption, commerce, real estate and public utilities.\nLong-term investment capital amounted to !6085.2 billion ($832 million) in\n1977. Of this amount !731.5 million ($100 million) was loaned to the agricultural sector. The Development Bank of the Philippines is the major source\nof long-term capital for agriculture. DHR estimates that only a small part of\nthis capitol will be available for `:naancing P/V systems.\nNational Electrification Administration (NEB? has been the source of funds\nfor the expansion of rural electric cooperatives. While there is currently no policy\ncommitting NEA to financing of P/V systems, it could be a possibility since\nthe purpose: of rural electrification and P/V is similar. The status of NEA\nconcessior.al loans as of December 1979 was nearly $1 billion, of which $218\nmillion was from international sources such as US AID, World Bank, France,\nNorway, etc. In 1980, concessional loans for wood-fired and mini-hydro power\nplants were obtained from French and Norwegian sources. The very low interest\nloans being granted by NEA will most certainly improve the economic viability\nof P/V systems. The criteria used in the\nselection were:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Level of production and importance of the product in the Philippines.\n^.:=\'\t\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Type of operation and its adaptability to use a P/V power source.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Extent of use of the operation in the Philippines.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Extent of the current level of mechanization of the operation (e.g..\nuse of conventional energy systems).\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Size of the power unit required for a typical operation.\nxiv\n\na\n\nThe applications cover a wide range of power requirements (2OW to 12XW\ncapacity), diverse load profiles and.varying operating environments. The\napplications cover fiah/prawn hatchery operations, irrigation, maintenance\nfacilities, grinding and milling, fish cultivation, salt productions ico\nmanufacture and agricultural extension services. The Philippines Agricultural Machinery Manufacturers\nand Dealers Association (AMHDA) data indicates that agricultural machinery\nInstallation rate (replacement plus new installations) amount to about 102\nper year of the existing stock. Most long-term financing in agriculture is\ndirected through the development banks, which are in turn partially financed\nthrough multi-lateral donor agencies, such as the Asian Development Bank and the\nWorld Bank, and bilateral agreements between the Philippines and donor countries.\nThese banks function not only as lending agencies but also as agents of government policy objectives. This dual function creates the need within the development banks to evaluate loans in terms of their economic benefits to the nation\nas well as their benefits to the potential applicant. An economic analysis\nmeasures the profit to the nation while a financial analysis measures the\nprofit to an investor of a project. Interviews with the Development Bank of\nthe Philippines planners indicated that the bank will in the future adopt economic\nanalysis of projects as part of their evaluation of loans. Once this practice\nhas been instituted, loans will be given according to the following criteria:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Loans would be given if projects pass both an economic and financial\nanalysis.\n\ns\t\n\ne If a project passes an economic analysis and not a financial analysis,\nit would be more likely to receive funding than if it passes a\nfinancial analysis and fails an economic analysis.\ne Finally, if a project fails both an economic and financial analysis,\nit will not receive funding.\nxvi\n\nConsidering that most long-term agricultural financing in the Philippines\nIs presently directed through the Development Sank of the Philippimse\nphotovoltaic firms wishing to sell to the goverment or to goroermwnt-financed\nprojects may have to justify on economic grounds the viability of these systems.\nMe in considering potential photovoltaic applications bas included in its\nevaluation of photovoltaic systems both an economic and a financial analysis.\nThe purpose of using both types of evaluations is to provide photovoltaic manufacturers with an idea of the economics under which these systems\nwill be financed by the development banks and private entrepreneurs in the Philippines.\nThe assumptions used in the economic and financial analyses are shown\nIn Table S and 6. Economic and financial life-cycle cost analyses were performed to determine how P/V systems would compete with conventionally powered\nsystems currently in use. The analyses assumed that. below 1.2KW capacity P/V systems\nwould compete with gasoline generators. The competitor for larger systems\nwould be diesel. Table 7 shows the year in which P/V systems would be costcompetitive with conventional systems. Used on economic analysis criteria, only applications\nsmaller than 2KWp are cost-competitive prior to 1987. Using financial analysis\ncriteria, mainly due to the higher effective fuel cost, systems lass than\n2KWp become cost-competitive by 1984.\nThese results imply that if P/V systems sales depend on government financing, only small systems would be eligible for loans in 1981-85 time-frame.\nThus the near-term market would be small. However. an examination of the types\nof low power applications show that many users of such systems could consider self-financing. These are applications used in corporate farms, or\noperations generating sufficiently large revenues. They include: flab stock\ngathering, transceivers. battery chargers for logging operations. forestry\nstations, maintenance yards, and fish/prawn hatcheries.\nMarket Assessment\n\nThe market size estimate is based on the hypothesis that a market will\nstart developing when P/V systems are cost-competitive, on # life-cycle\nbasis. when compared to its least-cost, practical alternat te. At this point,\nthe market share of P/V systems will be close to zero, as the conventional\nsystems have the added advantage of existing supply and repair facilities,\ntradition, smaller initial cost. and corresponding greater flexibility.\nAs the cost advantage of P/V systems grove so will its market share. We assume\nthat once the cost equality point is passed, the rate of penetration of P/V\nsystems will depend on other factors such as equipment turn-over rates, awareness, availability of finance, marketing strategies, availability of marketing\nchannels, and other market-related factors. We also assume that P/V systems\nwill first penetrate into areas where users are already using powered equipment. For example, they will be more acceptable to a farmers using a gasoline\nengine driven oump than one who irrigates the field manually.\nThe previous discussion showed that the size of the technically feasible\nmarket in the Philippines is large. However, there are several constraints\nto achieving this market. The most serious barriers are:\ne Awareness --There is, in general, a lack of awareness anong end-users,\nprivate and public banking and business communities, and government decision. makers as to the possibilities for P/V.\ne C^ st Competit i veness --P/V systems must be economically and financially\ncost-competitiv , with the least-cost practical alternative on a\nlife-cycle basis before its use will be seriously considered. Furthermore, Philippine consumers are very sensitive to first costs.\ne Financial --The availability of capital for long-term investments is\nvery small an(; will hinder the development of a substantial market.\n\nxix\n\nThe last constraint is, in our opinion, the most serious barrier to\ndeveloping a large P/V market.. Based on the above mentioned constraints and\t\nthe incentives described earlier, three market penetration estimates sre\ngiven. They are based or. the following scenarios:\n\ni\n\n"Stag o"--assumes that existing perceptions and attitudes of endusers, business community and government, persist and the level of\nactivity of P/V manufacturers is unchanged. It also assumes that the\nbasis for cost-competitiveness is an economic analysis based on the\n32 fuel cost escalation rate, since loans would be primarily from government sources with minimal private sector purchases of P/V systems.\n\n"increased Acttyity"- - assumes greater government interest, additional\n\ngovernment financing, aggressive marketing by P/V companies, and considerable interest and awareness. It also assumes that larger\noperations or those generating large revenues would self-finance P/V\nsystems. Thus cost-competitiveness is based on financial analyses\nusing a 3% fuel cost escalation rate.\n"Q&tJaL tU "--assumea a definite government commitment towards P/V,\npriority government financing, very strong interest and awareness,\nless affluent operations entering market, private sector financing,\nfinancial cost-competitive analysis, a greater fuel cost escalation\nrate, in addition to the incentives described in the previous scenarios.\n\nBased on the current level of financing in the agricultural sector, DHR\nestimates of capital available for P/V systems financing is shown in Table\nS. The estimates reflect the fact that there is a significant "learning"\nor awareness problem associated with P/V use and until that barrier is broken,\nno significant amounts of P/V systems will be installed. DHR estimates that\nIt will be at least 1985 before this barrier is minimized. The data in Table\n8 reflects only financing available from Filipino sources for commercial use\nof P/V, and does not take into account foreign funds supplied for demonstration\nprojects. Estimation of funds available for the latter activities is highly\nuncertain. The Non-Con Center\'s major role is technology development. but is increasingly involving itself in technology delivery and information dissemination\nactivities directed at users, especially in rural areas. The Non-Con Center has\ninitiated several P/V irrigation pumping experiments to evaluate their suitability\nfor use in Philippine conditions. Four small 200-25OWp demonstration irrigation\nsystems have been installed under the auspices of the United Nations Development\nProgram in cooperation with the Non-Con Center and the Farm Systems Development\nCorporation. Two of the systems were supplied by Solar Electric International.\na U.S. company. and the other two are French systems. The German government\nhas proposed a $4 million P/V system for electrifying a village. An agreement\nIn principle has been worked out between the German government. the Non-Con\nCenter and the Technology Resource Center. The system is expected to be in\nplace by the and of 1981. AEG-Telefunken will supply the P/V system and train\nFilipino technicians to operate and maintain the system. The military is also\nexperimenting with the use of P/V powered pumping systems for use in the\nsouthern part of the Philippines.\n\nxxi\nw\n\n{\n\nI\ni\n\nIn moving beyond experimentation and demonstration to commercialisation.\ngovernment energy experts are skeptical as to the appropriateness or m itch\nof P/V to their needs. Their major concern or reservation was based o;,, the\nhigh present market cost of P/V systems. This seemed to be the key inhibitor\nto a more widespread and higher level of interest necessary to mow beyond\ndemonstrations. They are taking a vast-and-sea attitude toward P/V system.\nOnly an improvement in the relative first cost of P/V to competing energy sources\nwill address this inhibiting factor. and create an atmosphere and possibility\nof increased interest. At that time. it will be possible to address remaining\nconcerns about P/V performance (a.$., reliability and durability) that can\nonly come with user experience in the field.\nEntrepreneurial Interest\nThere is currently an active and enthusiastic entrepreneurial interest\nIn photovoltaic* by Filipino businessmen. In interviews with both large and\nsmall industrialists, there was the attitude that photovoltaics could develop\nas a viable energy industry in the Philippines. Both a large industrial\ncorporation and a large commercial bank have expressed interest in financing\neconomically viable photovo]taics projects, as well as establishing a local\nphotovoltaic industry. Smaller firms, presently involved in selling flat\np ,te collectors. have also expressed an interest in establishing contact\nwith American photovoltaic manufacturers with the purpose of establishing\njoint manufacturing or marketing of photovoltaic systems.\nIn general. the level of knowledge and sophistication about potential\napplications, markets and photovoltaic systems is higher in larger firms. In\naddition. larger firms appeared to be interested in technology transfer\narrangements with the United States, while smaller entrepreneurs expressed\na willingness to act as dealers for photovoltaic systems. A number of entrepreneurs. both large and small, inquired about the possibility of setting up\na regional production facility in the Philippines to service the ASEAN market\n(Singapore. Thailand. Indonesia. Malaysia and the Philippines). These entrepreneurs felt that it may be possible to obtain preferential tariff duties\non an ASEAN photovoltaic facility from the ASEAN community. One firm\nhas advertised small photovoltaic systems in Philippine newspapers with\nlimited sales success. These systems were designed to power radios and\nelectric fans and are geared to the upper-middle and upper-class market. To-date\nthis businessman has not been able to penetrate this market successfully.\nOne problem (often cited businessmen) is the fact that potential customers\nare familiar with such larger systems. For example. farmers irrigating\ntheir fields commonly use S to 10 hp pumps. Smaller photovoltaic systems\n(below 1KW) are viewed with skepticism by farmers who feel that P/V cannot\ndo as good a job as a gasoline or diesel pump in irrigating their fields.\nTable10 summarises the current status of interest in F/V systems.\n\nxxii\n\n\t\n\nTABLE 10 PRESENT STATUS OF INTEREST IN P/V STSTEMS\n\nAM\t\n\nMile Moor\t\n\n\xe2\x96\xbatesstt states\no Lack of tafetsstion as Ph y soverneset Msislos-eskers.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Wit and sae attitude tewtds Ph y asssq officials.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 sbaptieiw by planners that Ph could easpoto with blow\nand blesses.\n\nPrivate sector\t\n\no Active and estbuslastic ontreprawrtal interest is P/Y by\nFilipino buslaeswea.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Interest by Velvets bwisass gs is establishing costm.ts eitb\nM sssufactarsrs is the V.S.\n\nBusiness Environment for Marketing P/V Systems\nPresently. They include:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Stated government policy towards promotion of renewable. and\ndispersed power sources.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Financial incentives for the production and use of P/V systems.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 High cost of conventional energy supplies such as gasoline and diesel.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Entrepreneurial interest in P/V systems.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Cost-competitiveness of P/V systems when compared to gasoline and\ndiesel power sources in a wide range of agricultural applications.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Availability of suitable marketing channels for sale of P/V systems\nIn the agricultural sector.\n\'\t\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 The shortage of skilled labor for maintaining competing systems.\n3Miv\n\nHowever, wry significant and serious barriers prevent achieving the\ntechnically feasible, coat-competitive market for P/V systems in the agricultural sector. These include:\ne The lack of awareness of the possibilities for P/V systems.\ne The shortage of capital for financing P/V systems.\ne High first costs of P/V when compared to competing systems.\nThus, if the status quo is maintained, the market for P/V power systems\nin the Philippine agricultural sector will be scull. The mast ._ t_\ntee\nr\nreason for the limited market is the availability of capital for financint\nP/V s stang . If this constraint could be relaxed, then the cost-effective\nmarket becw:.es larger by several orders of magnitude. Thus devising Innovative financing schemes and promotional campaigns could ha\xc2\xab a very high\npayoff. The market would be mainly small power applications in the pre-1985\nperiod with sales to affluent individuals or corporations. In the post-1985\nperiod the market for larger systems could open-up if long-term financing is\nprovided by the Philippine banking community.\n\nA\n\nf\n\n^\t\n\niii&\n\nxxlt\n\n\'.\t\n\nMAR1<ET ASSESSl1E11T OF PHOTOVOLTAIC PGMiR\n\nRMM\n\nAGRICULTURAL APILICA?IOIIS m3am\nISFORT NO. I\n\nTHE PHILIPPIN&B\n\n1.0 INTRODUCTION\n1.1 Sackaround\nThe United States National Photovoltaic (P/V) Program has been established\nby the U.S. Department of Energy (U.S. DOE) to bring P/V power systems to the\neconomic marketing stage where they will be able to contribute significau \xe2\x96\xbatly\nto the U.S. energy requirements by the and of this decade. Ongoing research,\ndevelopment and demonstrations are directed at achieving major system cost\nreductions and field experiences with P/V power systems. The program is\nmanaged by the U.S. DOE and consists of several project offices, one of which\nIs the Photovoltaic Stand-Alone Applications Project Office at NASA Lewis\nResearch Center, Cleveland, Ohio. this project office is conducting international market assessments to ascertain whether stand-alone P/V power\nsystems can provide useful and economically productive power for various\napplications in developing countries, during the next several years.\n1.2 ObJectives\nThe Photovoltaic Stand-Alone Applications Project Office of NASA/Lewis\nResearch Center (NASA/LeRC), Cleveland, Ohio has sponsored a contract to conduct an assessment of the potential for remote photovoltaics (P/V) power\nsystems in worldwide agriculture. the types of potential photovoltaic applications considered in the contract are &-hose requiring less than 150 of power\nand operating in stand-alone configuration without back-up power. These\napplications include: irrigation, post-harvest operations, food and fiber\nprocessing and storage, and livestock and fisheries operations. A team of\nD11R. Incorporated and Associates in Rural Development, Inc. visited the\n1-1\n\n\t\n\n\tk\'\n\ni\nPhilippines, October to\n\n\t\nE i\t\nI\n\nNovember, 1980. The primary pupi" ef\'this Philippine\n\nreport is to provide an assessment of the market for stand-aline photovoltaic\nsystems in Philippine agriculture.\nSince this contract intends to identify applications with high P/V sales\npotential so that photovoltaic suppliers and distributors could dstU* W.\npropriate marketing strategies, this analysis provides the followbW essential\n\nmarket information for the Philippines:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Estimates of pctential market size for P/V power applications\nin the agriculture sector.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Power requirements and usage profiles for a variety of agricultural\napplications which are compatible with, a P/V system.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 operating and cost Characteristics of gasoline and diesel power\nsystems that will compete against P/V.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Energy, agriculture and development goals, programs and\npolicies which will influence P/V sales.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Appropriate financing mechanisms and capital available for\nP/V system purchases.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Channels for distribution, installation and maintenance of\nP/V systems.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Appropriate methods for conducting business, in the country.\nin addition to the data collection activities, the team members gave presentations on P/V energy systems, and their current applications. They also\ndistributed sets of brochures consisting of advertising material obtained\nfrom P/V companies and from U.S. Government sources.\nThe types of applications being considered in this assessment are those\n\nrequiring leis than 15M of power and operating in a stand -alone configuration\nwith no conventional back-up power source.\n1.3\n\nStudy Approach\n\nThe DUR study approach consists 0 a focused data collection visit to the\ncountry followed by a detailed analysis and a market assessmtnt . bassd on this\ndata. The three percent annual average\ngrowth rate has resulted in a population whose median age is 17-18\nyears. The per capita gross national product (GNP) is about $500\n(U. S.). Nearly one-tenth of the population lives in the capital\nof Manila and its suburbs, and another two-tenths reside in other\nurban areas. Seventy percent of the population is classified as\nrural.\nFifty percent of employment is within agriculture, fisheries,\nand forestry. However, this sector contributes only 25 percent\nof the GNP, as nearly seven out of 10 farmers are rice and/or maize\nsemi-sufficient farmers. Less than one-third of the land is considered\narable, and of the 11 million hectares of the land devoted principally\nto agriculture, 75 percent of it is badly eroded. The average farm\nsize has been estimated at close to\n\nA\n\nhectares.\n\nThe Philippines is an archipelago, about the size of Arizona,\nthat is divided into three island groups: Luzon, Mindanao, and\nVisayas. Although it consists of 7,100 islands, 11 of them comprise\nin area more than 95 percent of the country\'s total area. Luzon,\nin the north, is the largest island. At present, central Luzon\nis the nation\'s rice bowl, and the bulk of irrigated land occurs\nt\t\nt\n\nin this section. However, by the year 2000, western and northern\nMindanao are expected to provide the majority of the nation\'s rice\nneeds. Mindanao is the second largest island and lies in the south.\n\ni,\n2-1\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\t\n\xe2\x80\xa2\t\n\nThe other nine islands, the Visayan, constitute 29 percent of the\ns total area of the country. The northern regions (Ilocos, Cagayan\nValley, and central Luton), the western Visayan, and !Mindanao are\nthe most important areas of intensive crop farming.\nLarge-scale operations produce sugar, bananas, and pineapple\nfor export, and are increasingly moving into poultry and swine production.\nSmall-holders and subsistence farmers primarily produce food grains\nfor domestic use and coconut for export. Although the farm population\nis more or less equally divided among regions, man-land ratios and\nfarm size differences are quite marked. Regions with the most cultivable\nland resources per person are the Cagayan Valley, central Mindanao, and\nto a less degree, western Mindanao. fSee Appendix F).\n2.1 Energy Situation Overview\nEnergy demand has been increasing steadily in the Philippines\nover the last two decades. In the mid-sixties, average per capita\nannual energy consumption was the equivalent of 1.1 barrels of oil,\nbut had risen by 1980 to two barrels. ! Domestic oil, geothermal, coal, hydroelectric# and\na number of other renewable and nonconventional energy resources\nare viewed as major possibilities for helping reduce oil importr\nand are presently under development.\ne DOMESTIC OIL -- Extensive exploration and oil production\nhave only recently begun in the Philippines. However, initial\nhopes during the late 1970s that oil production would climb\nabove 10 million barrels annually, and provide eight percent\nor more of the national energy supply, are now being questioned.\no GEOTHERMhL -- The Philippines has an extensivc geothermal\ndevelopment program underway, and this energy source has\nhigh potential for greatly increasing the electrical energy\nsupply and for changing the energy supply six. During the\npast eight years, geothermal power has increased from approximately\n10 MW of capacity to about 1,200 MW of potentially feasible\nproduction. This resource does vary considerably, however,\namong the major islands, as it is not present on all nor\nis it equally distributed where it is present.\nCOAL -- Domestic coal production has also increased quite\nPast\neight year* to approximatedramatically, up eightfold in the\nly 300,000 metric tons. According to Philippine government\nprojections, further incroases in production (and increased\nexploration of new reserves) will increase the volume of\ncoal use from 2.4 percent of total commerical energy consumption\nin 1981 to 13.4 percent by 1985.\ne HYDROELECTRIC -- There is still considerable potential for\ntapping hydroelectric power in the Philippines. This potential\nvaries greatly frtv reqion to region and from island to\nisland. further information on locations and production\nprojections are given in section 3.2 on rural electrification.\nMini-hydroel*ctric system are also a major fo c us of recent\nactivity and are listed in Table 2-2 under nonconv*ntional\nenergy sources.\n\n2-5\n\ni\ni\nC\ni\n\ni\n\ne OTHER RENEWABLE AND NOKVNVENTIONAL RESOURCES -- Table 2-2\nshows the Ministry of Energy\'s supply projections for what\nthey consider to be the key technologies and sources for\nthe Philippines. Of potential competitive importance is\nthe development of the use of marsh gas/natural gas emanations,\nwind, agricultural w&stes, or producer gas technologies.\nAlthough they are not yet in widespread use, potential development of these technologies is a factor for site-specific\napplications. where the government and the private sector\nare experimenting with their feasibility and performance.\nOf particular importance tn the potential P/V market is the\ndevelopment of bio-gas technology, which, for example, can\nbecome a direct competitor with P/V in poultry, swine-raising,\nor feedlot operations.\nAn energy supply plan presently under development is the\ngrowing of Ipil-Ipil. a fast growing tree. for dendro-thermal\npower plants. Another program is the ongoing effort to develop\nan alcohol fuel program using sugarcane and casava as feedstocks.\nThe Rovernment and the private sector are pursuing this option\nwith p roduction and sales scheduled for 1981.\nImplications of the Energy \xe2\x96\xba situation and Government Enera_y Plans\nfor Pa systems\nThe widespread awareness of what the cost of imported oil is\ndoing to the Philippine economy and the desire to seek and develop\nalternatives, provide a situation where P/V could be of benefit and\ninterest. However, the government has not included a significant\nrole for P/V in their five-year energy plan. The main rationale\nand thrust of government efforts is to apply well-known and costAffective technologies, and to wait for tho more industrialised\nand developed countries to develop and bring down the price of more\nexperimental or newer technologies. P/V systems are clearly viewed\nin this latter category in the official government energy plans.\nHowever. although the projected role for\n\n?IV systems in the\n\nPhilippines is low in total peak kilowatts when compared to present\nworldwide\n\n?IV production levels and sales. the Philippine market\n\npotential is significant.\n\n2-6\n\ni\n\nTable 2-2\n\n1M1tGV\t\n\nIstma"\t\n!N[aGY szrMMs\n(In thouaand barrel"NO equivalent. These decrees\nmade regulatory. research and development, and planning functions the\nbroad responsibility of the Ministry of snarly.\nCrowing awareness of the serioutness of the enatty probleme\nIts potential for inhibiting the development plans of the Philippines,\nand the already strong negative effects on the Philippine economy,\nled the government in 1960 to compress its ten-year energ y plan to\nfive years. 1981 to 1985. The intent in compreasing the plan is to\nspeed up the development of indigenous energy resources and the adoption\nof energy strategies that will sake the Philippines increasingly selfsufficient.\nFigure, 2-2 shows the organizational chart for the Ministry of Energy\nas it existed in late 1980. Due to the focus of the government on the\ndevelopment of hydroelectric goethermal and domestic fossil enerlr; resources\nand the rural electrification program, the pTimary agencies for formulating\nenergy policy and financing energy projects Include the Philippine National\nOil Company. National Power Corporation, the National Electrification Adminiatration. and the National Mural Electrification Cooperative Association.\nResponsibilities and roles of these agencies is discussed in action 3.2 on\nrural electrification. in addition, energy-related research is being\ncarried out by the University of the Philippines in Quezon City and at Los\nSmos by the National Institute of Science and Technology. Roles and\nactivities of those agencies most directly involved in agriculture-related\nIssues are discussed in section 3.3\n\n27or example. Although several other government agencies\nare strongly pursuing renewable energy projects and policies, the Non-Con\nCenter is presently responsible for research and develo pment work, and coordination of P/V demonstration projects ( See Figure 3.2).\nAs stated previously, P/V is not scheduled for a significant role\nin the government energy plan. However, the Non-Con Center is presently\ncarrying out a small applied research program with P/V systems supplied\nby Solar Electric International and the French. Involved with this\nprogram is UNDP support of a P /V-powered small-scale water pumping demonstration project. The Philippine military has also installed several P/Vpowered systems in southern Mindanao. Also, the Federal Republic of\nGermany is supporting an experimental demonstration of a P/V-powered\nvillage system ( 15 kwp) tc be installed by AEG - Telefunken. However, as of\nthe Philippine visit by the DHR team, the arrays had not yet been installed\nin the field and exact siting was uncertain.\n\nE\n\n2-10\n\nFIGURE 2.3\n\nNoncon Center Organization\nMINISTER OF ENERGI\n\nASSISTA\\T\nSECRETARY\n\nl\n\nCE\\TER FOR \\0\\(O\\\\E\\TIO\\kl\nE\\ERG1 DE% ELOP\\IE\\T\n\n\\LD\nRE(,IO\\aL\n1+P \\\\( HFti\n\n"c\\GI\\cERI\\G`\nI L 1BOR ATOR1\n\nnl\\ IcION J\n\nAl l RLD11 EU\n\\O\\CO\\\nCF\\TFRS\n\n&,DN11\\IcTR ATnR\n\nDIVISI\n\nLAR\nDI V ISIOti\nLS ) I (0 D V SIIONGA\n\nLD\n\nNOLOGI QD1HNOLOG\nOPMEJS7 ESSME%I\nVIS10%\nISION\t\n\n(\n\n7DWNISTRATI^ E\t\nDID ISIO\\\t\n\n2-11\n\nFI\\A%CE\nDIVISIO\\\n\nWATER POWER\nDIVISION\n\nT1ECHNOLOGl\n\nPROMOT10%\n\nDIVISION .\n\nSPECIAL\nPROJECTS\nDIVISIn\\\n\n3.0 PHILIPPINE DEVEIAPNW PLANS\n3.1 Economic Development Plans\nThe.modern industrial sector, --j used primarily of manufacturing,\nconstruction, and mining and quarrying, is heavily concentrated\naround Manila and accounts for about one-third of gross domestic\nproduct. The large, mostly traditional agriculture\'and service sectors\ncontribute the remaining two-thirds. Overall economic growth has\naveraged approximately six percent annually since 1970. In agriculture,\nthis was mainly due to increases in rice an* corn production.I\nThe economy is still heavily dependent upon the export of a few\nprimary goods, i.e., sugar, coconut products, copper ores, and forestry\nproducts, for its foreign exchange earnings. Nontraditional exports,\nsuch as bananas and pineapples, however, have become increasingly\nimportant. Trade deficits have averaged one billion dollars for the\npast five years. This is roughly equivalent to the cost of crude oil\nimports, which represent more than one-fourth of the total imports. The\nincreasing cost of oil-based energy, which represents 90 percent of the economy\'s\nenergy requirements, is helping to fuel inflation at a current rate of\n2\n13 percent.\nThe government believes that two key issues must be addressed\nby their overall development plan. 3 First, with a rapidly increasing\n\n1Ste "Country Development Strategy, FY 1982 -- Philippines," Agency for\nInternational Development.\nThis is an official Philippines government estimate of the inflation rate\nin 1980.\n3These overall goals are taken directly from the "Ten-Year Philippine Development\nPlan, 1978-1987", and the DHR team did find commitment among government officials and programs to the general thrust of the plan.\n3-1\n\nwork force, there is a need to find productive work for people. Second#\nthere exists the pressing need to correct the negative balance of payments situation. Consequently, the present government policy consists\nof two main components:\n1) mobilization of the rural sector to expand labor-intensive\nagricultural production and labor-intensive small- and\nmedium-scale industry to serve rural demands and,\n2)\n\nexpansion of labor-intensive export products to earn\nneeded foreigr exchange.\n\nThe government has stated that capital-intensive and high-risk\nactivities, or those of a service nature, will be the government\'s main\nconcerns. As is discussed in later sections of this report, the role of\nthe private sector is vital in the government\'s economic development\nplans, and is clearly considered a primary actor in both energy and agricultural development activities. In sum, the government has adopted a\nstrategy of developing energy self-sufficiency through increasing reliance\non indigenous and renewable sources of energy, and has coupled this with a\npolicy of increasing agricultural production and efficiency of fossil fuel\nuse in most agricultural activities. Increasing energy self-sufficiency\nand agricultural production are to be brought about within the framework\nof developing more rural areas.\n3.2 Rural Electrification\nThe Philippines has been rapidly accelerating their rural electrification program and view it as a major and important development goal.\nThe aim of the government and the power development program is the total\nelectrification of the country by 1985. within this plan, electricity\ngeneration and transmission is the responsibility of the National Power\nCorporation (NAPOCOR), and the organization of electrical cooperatives is\ncarried out by the National Electrification Administration (NEA) for power\ndistribution and retail.\n3-2\n\n\t\n\nThe power sector in the Philippines consists of over 800 public and\nprivate utilities, the majority of which either own and operate generating\nunits of less than 10 !W capacity, or purchase and distribute power for \t\n\nN\n\nbase load requirements from HAPOCOR, the biggest single power -producing\nutility in the country. Average national energy availability from all\n\n^.\t\n\npower utilities was estimated in 1980 to be 3,301 W. Small private utilities and municipal systems provide power in many areas not covered by the\npresently developed grid networks. Table 3-1 shows the installed gene.\t\n\nrating capacity and the projections for 1985.\nThe NEA involves itself mainly in the electrification of rural areas,\nleaving the private sector to cover urban areas. Over the next five years,\nNEA will be concentrating on the distribution of power generally made\navailable by NAPOCOR. This is to be accomplished primarily through the\nformation of cooperatives. In 1979, NEA set up six additional cooperatives,\nmaking the total of registered cooperatives 116. Table 3-2 shows the status \t\n\nI\ni\n\nof electrification in the NEA cooperative structure as of early 1980.\nFigures 3-1, 3-2, and 3-3 show the present and planned generating\ncapacity that will provide power for the rural electrification program. \t\n\n!\nS\n\nPart of the goverrunent \' s change in the new five -year energy plan has been\nto emphasize geothermal, hydroelectric, and coal plants as a move away\nfrom oil. NEA provides capital loans to cooperatives connects-d to the\nNAPOCOR grid at three percent interest for a 25-year term with a five-year\ngrace period. For self-generating cooperatives, the interest rate NEA\ngives is two percent with a 25-year loan term and five-year grace period.\ni\t\n\nTable 3-3 shows the cost in dollars of installing distribution lines and\nof hookups.\n\n3-3\n\nOW\n\nTabl\xe2\x80\xa2 3-1\n\nINSTALLED GENERATING CAPACMZ$ BY It9ClgN\n\n(In rnegawatts, MA stated that 30 percent\nof the rural cooperatives operate an average of 24 hours a day, and that\nsame operate only about six hours a day due to the cost of oil.\nAs already stated, the aim of the goverment and the power development\nprogram is the total electrification of the country by 1985. The country\nis presently considered to be approximately 4S-SO percent electrified.\nHowever, almost all of this is in metropolitan areas or towns, and little\nhas reached into rural areas. of importance to P/V use is that it is\nnecessary to interpret what 100 percent electrification will mean in actual\ndelivery of electrical power to rural areas. The Philippines is using a\nbackbone grid system and plans to connect secondary lines to the backbone\nat lator times. This "100 percent" figure for rural areas means only that\nthe backbone will be in main towns and villages. Many smaller villages,\nislands, and rural areas may remain unelectrified into the 21st Century.\nThis fact was repeatedly confirmed by the MM team in discussions in the\nPhilippines.\nThere are two further facts concerning the meaning of 100 percent\nelectrification that may impact upon the potential P/V market. First,\nmost electrical distribution systems in the Philippines experience\nnumerous breakdowns or brownouts, with resulting unreliable service.\nThis is especially true in the more rural areas. In fact, most of the\nlarger generators sold to industry in the Philippines are intended for\nuse as backup generators. Second, due both to lack of reliability and,\nin sane areas, the high price of electrical power, the ORR tea\xc2\xae found\nthat some consumers involved in agriculturally related activities who had\naccess to grid power preferred to buy a gasoline or diesel generator.\n3-10\n\nIn summary, P/V systems will have to ccopete with grid power for\nthose agriculturally-related processes# e.g., rice milling that can be\nlocated near the towns or villages that will be connected. however, even\nin these instances, P/V systems might occasionally be the preferred power\nsource due to unreliability or to the cost of electricity produced by\noil-generating plants. The grid will not be accessible to many field\noperations and to many of the smaller villages, probably until the 21st\nCentury.\n3.3 Agriculture\nThe major crops for which increased production is being strongly\nsupported are rice and corn. Secondarily, programs in cattle development,\ndairy, and cotton are considered of critical importance, although production of these products has not been increasing as fast as intended.\nTable 3-4 is taken from the government\'s development plan, 1978-1982,5\nand indicates the key components of ongoing government programs.\nHowever, there are several important development policies and\nprograms within the government agricultural sector that relate specifically to opportunities for P/V systems. The crops discussed below are\nchosen due to their importance for P/V applications throughout all\nproduction phases of the crop.\n\n\t\n9\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 RICE -- Though the Philippines has reached self-sufficiency\nin rice, a 4.4 percent annual increase in rice production\nhas been targeted for the 1978-1982 period to keep up with\npopulation growth and provide an adequate buffer stock. Of\nmore importance. to P/V applications is the attempt by the\ngovernment and farmers to reduce use of fossil fuels and\n\n5 1*Five-Year Development Plan, 1978-1982".\n\n3-11\n\nTable 3-4\nPriority Programs and Projects\n\nProgram/Project\t\n1.\t Production Programs\n1. It aims to formulate an\nintegrated program of action for the amelioration\nof the living conditions of set t ler families it Ownno, Nueva Vizcaya and Southern Leyte.\n\nNFAC - National Fcod and Agricultural Council\nNGA - National Grains Authority\nPHILSUCOM - Philippine Sugarcane Commission\nPCA - Philippine Coconut Authority\nBAI - Bureau of Animal Industry\nLDC - Livestock Development Council\nBFAR - Bureau of Fisheries and Aquatic Resources\nPCC - Philippine Cotton Cooperative\nDAR - Department of Agrarian Reform\n\n3-la\n\nDAR\n\nnonrenewable energy in rice production and processing.\nImproved irrigation and methods of water pumping are\na continuing priority. However, there have been increased\ncrop losses recently due to nonuse of powered irrigation,\nas farmers have not wanted to pay the increased cost of\ngasoline.\nNational Grains Authority (NGA) officials stated to the\nDHR team that, by international standards, about 60 percent\nof the rice crop is often of lower quality due to inadequate\ndrying. In addition to increasing the number of farmers\nusing dryers, the NGA has instructed their research and\ndevelopment units to attempt to eliminate, wherever possible,\nthe need for fossil fuel in drying and other operations for\nrice and corn. NGA officials stated that they did not see\nrural electrification as providing any major help in solving\ntheir energy needs in field operations.\ne CORN -- Increasing corn production for human consumption is\na major emphasis at this time due to a large domestic production deficit, and the resulting importation of about\n120,000 metric tons per year. Moreover, the key limiting\nfactor to increasing animal productio: in the Philippines\nis the lack of feed grains. Production is targeted to\nincrease by 20 to 25 percent over the next six years. This\nincreased production is meant to reduce the need for imported\ncorn and to increase the availability of corn as an animal\nfeed.\nIn the drier areas of the Philippines, corn can be rotated\nwith rice during the dry season, as it requires much less\nwater, even though still needing irrigation. This strategy\nis being supported by the Ministry of Agriculture and is\nexpected to spread. Also, better post-harvest drying methods\nare being encouraged because losses are sometimes as high as\n60 to 90 percent due to poor drying and infestation during\nstorage. Methods where P/V is applicable are discussed in\nchapter 5.\ne OTHER CROPS -- There are several crops for which P/V does\nnot seem a likely power source in actual production.\nHowever, P/V could be utilized in support facilities and\nat central building complexes on large farms. Sugarcane,\nbananas, pineapple, and casava are crops in this category.\nBanana and pineapple production should grow slightly for\nithe export market. Sugarcane and casava may experience more\nrapid growth in feedstocks for alcohol fuel production. If\nthe Philippine g overnment develops a large alcohol program,\nthen production of these crops will rise dramatically. As\nyet, however, there is no indication of what increased\nproduction is likely to occur.\n\n1\t\n\n3-15\n\nThere are a number of other crops in the Philippines that\nare either not experiencing increases in production or\nare not, at this time, likely candidates for P/V system\napplications in production operations. Among these are\ncoffee, rubber, cocoa, coconut, tobacco, wheat, and cotton.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 LIVESTOCK PRODUCTION -- Poultry, pork, beef, and dairy are\nall priority production areas. Government policies support\na number of programs aimed at improving production efficiency\nand increasing the size of operations. This is especially\ntrue with poultry and pork operations where the private sector\nis extremely active in establishing larger and more efficient\noperations. However, at this time, the number of P/V applications in livestock production seems limited.\n\ni\n\n3.4 Fisheries\t\n\nThe fishing industry in the Philippines employs approximately\n795,000 fishermen and has been contributing about five percent annually\nto the GNP. Ob3ectives of the government policy are:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 attain and maintain self-sufficiency in fish;\n\xe2\x80\xa2 optimize the use of fish and other a q uatic resources, and\nreduce wastage;\n\xe2\x80\xa2 promote import substitution and increase the exportation\nof traditional and nontraditional fish and fishery products;\nand\n\xe2\x80\xa2 achieve and maintain the optimum productive condition of\nthe country\'s fishery resources.\nThe strategies for the exploitation of fishery resources are to:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 increase yields of existing production units in capture\nfisheries and aquaculture, and selectively expand production\nunits;\n\xe2\x80\xa2 improve the fish marketing and distribution systems to minimize\ngaps between production and consumption on the local and\nregional levels, and to retain the quality of fresh fish\nand fishery products that reach consumers;\n\xe2\x80\xa2 develop processed products from indigenous fishery resources\ntraditionally exported in raw or semi-processed forms;\n\nThese goals are from the "Integrated Fisheries Development Plan",\nBureau of Fisheries and Aquatic Resources, 1980.\n\n3-16\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 develop fishery resource-based cottage industries and other\nancillary industries;\n\xe2\x80\xa2 develop local markets for import substitutes, and traditional\nand nontraditional foreign markets, for fish and fishery\nproducts;\n\xe2\x80\xa2 protect the fishery resources fiom illegal practices of\nover-exploitation and pollution by both domestic and foreign\nvessels; and\n\xe2\x80\xa2 promote the use of appropriate technologies for the exploitation\nand use of the resources.\nThe highest projected growth rate in fisheries is 10.8 percent\nfor inland fisheries. This estimate is of importance, as the DHR\nteam identified inland fisheries as the most likely part of the\nfisheries sector for P/V applications. Electricity is presently\nused (mainly for water pumping and aeration) in hatching operations\nand in fish ponds to allow for increasing the stocking density.\nThe Bureau of Fisheries and Aquatic Resources has an ongoing inland\nfisheries and aquaculture development program.\nThe incremental production in inland fisheries is expected\nto come from the increased production of existing and additional\nfreshwater and brackish water fish ponds (both government leased\nand privately owned), fish pond estates in suitable areas, and lake\nfisheries (including fish pen and cage culture). The major activities\ndesigned to realize the targeted incremental production and development\nof this specific project are fingerling production and dispersal,\nextension services, research, fish pond development surveys, and\ntraining for the private sector.\n3.5 Forestry,\nThe major effort in forestry in the Philippines is directed\nat reforestation, and at manrging and protectin; the existing forest_\n\n3-17\n\nresources, rather than increasing harvests. Forest resources came\nunder increasingly heavy pressure during the 1970s, and government\npolicy is mainly directed at assuring a long-term production base.\nOf particular interest to the energy sector is the development\nof Ipil-Ipil plantations\' The Forest Products Research and Industries\nDevelopment Commission is involved with the Ministry of Natural\nResources regarding wood production research and field production\nstrategies. Initial planting has already occurred. The National\nElectrification Administration is managing the overall effort of\ngenerating electricity using wood-fired plants. Two pilot plants\nhave been initiated: one 500 kw plant and another two to five MW\nplant. The intended users of this power are rural electrical cooperatives.\n\n`\t\n\n6Leucaena - Promising Forage and Tree Crops for the Tropics,\nNational Academy of Sciences, Washington, D.C., 1977.\n\n3-16\n\n4.0 FINANCING OF ENERGY, AGRICULTURE AND MVELOPIMM PROJECTS\n4.1 Overview of the Philippines Banking/Investment System\nThe financial system of the Philippines is a network of bank and\nnon-bank financial institutions. The banking sector is composed of the\ncommercial banks, thrift banks including savings and mortgage banks, rural\nbanks, development banks and the savings and loan associations. The nonbank sector includes investment houses, insurance and finance companies,\npension and trust funds and pawnshops. The Central Bank of the Philippines\nregulates the operation of bank and non-bank financial institutions. The\nbanking sector accepts deposits, grants loans, invests in equities, rediscounts papers and acts as trustee. In actual practice, however, each\nfinancial institution specializes in certain types of credit service.\nComplementing the financial system are specialized banks comprising o: the\nDevelopment Bank of the Philippines, the Land Bank, and the Philippine\nAmanah Bank as well as government non-bank financial institutions like the\nSocial Security System, the Government Service Insurance System, the Agricultural Credit Administration and the National Investment and Development\nCorporation.\nOf the financial institutions mentioned above, six are primarily\nconcerned with investment in energy, agriculture and development projects.\nThese six financial institutions are:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Commercial Banks which are active in small loans to small\nand medium industries. These loans are short-term and have\nhigh interest rates.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Savings and Mortgage Banks that grant loans in a similar\nfashion to commercial banks but cannot engage in foreign\nexchange trading, nor open letters of credit.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Rural Banks which are privately awned like commercial and\nsavings banks but receive financial and technical assistance\nand incentives from the government. Rura l banks primarily\nprovide short-term working capital loans to farmers.\n\n4-1\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Land Sank of the Philippines which is owned by the government and is\ncharged with the responsibility of financing the transfers of land\nfrom landlords to tenant-farriers as well as redirecting land-owner\nresources to industry or other productive endeavors.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Development banks which usually provide short to long-terra financing for\nproductive enterprises on a secured basis. These loans are given\nto both industry and agriculture. These banks are government owned.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Non-Sank Financial Institutions which extend financial services to the\ncommercial and industrial sectors of the economy. Financing is\nshort- to long-term in nature.\nA more detailed description of the above institutions can be found in\nAppendix D.\n\n4.2 Attitudes of Financial institutions Towards Photavoltaics\nInterviews with a number of private and government financial institutions\nrepresentatives were held to assess the attitudes of the financial community\ntowards photovoltaic systems. As a whole there was a general sense of\nenthusiasm and willingness of high officials to consider photovoltaics\nsystems for loans. At the project staff level , !/ however, there was a general\nsense of skepticism as to photovoltaics\' applicability in Philippines agriculture. This skepticism appeared to be influenced by the following factors:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 High initial capital cost of photovoltaic systems\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Awareness b; project staff that only small photovoltaic systems are\ncurrently economical\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Lack of information by project staff on photovoltaics\' limitations\nand advantages\n\xe2\x80\xa2 A belief that photovoltaics will not, in the near future, be able to\nsupply large quantities of power at a low cost\n^.\t\n\nf\n1\n\nt\nL\n\nr\n\n1/ Those technical/economic analysts responsible for evaluating and recommending projects for loans.\n4-2\n\nT\'\n\nY\n\ntt\t\n\nJ+\n\n11\n\ne A belief that other energy sources, such as biogas, should be\nfinanced before photovoltaic*.\nAlthough project staff remained skeptical after being interviewed, there\nappeared to be a consensus that they would consider photovoltaic* as one\nenergy alternative in a project and would finance photovoltaic projects if\nthey proved to be economically and financially viable.\n4.3 Availability of Long-Term Investment Funds\nIn the Philippines, investment funds can be categorized into mediumand long-term loans (2 to 5 years for medium-term loans and 5 to 15 years\nfor long-term loans) and short-term loans (less than 2 year loan periods).\nDue to high inflation rates and rapidly changing interest rates most of the\ncapital loaned by commercial and savings banks tend to be short-term in\nnature. Capital loaned by the development banks, both private and government\nowned, rural banks, and the Land of Bank of the Philippines tend to be in the\nform of medium and long-term loans. Of these long-term lending institutions,\nonly the private and government development banks, and non-bank financial\ninstitutions as a rule issue long-term loans for fixed assets in excess of\nP50,000. Rural banks typically issue small working capital loans while the\nLand of Bank of the Philippines financts primarily land transfers from owners\nto tenants.\nCapital for long-term loans by the development banks is usually\nborrowed from either the central banks of donor countries, bilateral aid\nagencies such as the United States Agency for International Development,\nmultilateral aid agencies such as the world Bank and Asia Development Bank,\nor export financing banks such as the U.S. Export-Import Bank. As Table\n4.1 illustrates, the Development Bank of the Philippines total long-term\nForeign Borrowings amounted to over $992 million in 1979. Of this total,\nloans directly from the United States or agencies financed by the United\nStates totaled $337 million or nearby one third of total foreign borrowings.\n\n4-3\n\nf\n\n^t\n\n,\n\nTable 4.1 Long -Term Foreign Borrowings from MAID,\nWorld Bank, Export-Import Bank, U.S.\nCommodity Credit Corporation, and Asia\n\nDevelopsent Bank for the Development\nBank of the Philippines\n\n`\t\n\nForei gn Borrowings\n\nTotal Long-Term Foreign Borrowings\n\n$992,279,653\n\nU.S. AID 41% US $ loan, due 1968-1968\n\n4,860,669\n\nU.S. AID 344 USS loan, due 1974 - 1994\n\n3,460,000\n\nExport-Import Bank of the US, 64 US$ loan\n\n54,965,158\n\ndue 1974-1964\n\nU.S. Commodity Credit Corporation, 84 to 104\nUS$ credit, due 1975-1981\n\n105,938,000\n\nIBRD, through Philippine Government 714, due\n\n48,000,000\n\n1979-1992\n\nIBRD, through Philippine Government, 84 due\n\n20,000,000\n\n1978-1992\n\nIBRD, through Philippine Government 744, due\n\n50,000,000\n\n1961-1995\n\nIBRD, through Philippine Government 8j4, due\n\n506000.000\n\n1960-1993\n$337,224,027\n\nTOTAL U.S. BORROWING \t\n\n\t\nF\n\n4-4\n\n3\n\n..\n\n% "-V^ fit r -1 -\n\nTable 4.2 illustrates the relative size of total loans and investments outstanding of the major Philippine financial institutions.\nCcssmsrcisl banks accounts for 72% or over P59 billion of outstanding\n\nf\ncapital in the Philippines as of June, 1976. and repair of\nmachinery and equipment (Table 4.4).***\n4.4 Availability of Long-Term Investment Funds for Photovoltaics Systems\nin Agriculture\nFor the most part, photovoltaic systems, due to their high initial\ncapital cost, will require long-team capital financing. The previous section\nestimated the amount of available, long-term investment capital in the Philippines in 1977 to be approximately 26085.2 billion of which F731.5 million\nwas allocated to agriculture (both foreign and domestic). In 1979, the DBP\nloaned 2424.4 million ($60 million) for domestic and 8277 million ($31 million)\nfor foreign agricultural projects. In contrast, the long-term loans given\nto cooperatives for rural electrification in 1979 amounted to 9917 million\n($125 million). These figures indicate that the amount of long-term capital\nis small and would not be available to finance large purchases of photovoltaic\nsystems. It is clear that long-term financing photovoltaic systems in\nagriculture would fall under the lending program of the DBP due to the institution\'s major contribution to the long-term capital market in the Philippines.\nCommercial and savings banks, although the largest source of capital in the\nPhilippines, would be interested primarily in short-term working capital\nloans for low-risk, familiar, technologies. This preference tends to work\nagainst photovoltaic systems. The amount of long-term capital available for\nagricultural projects appears to have been less than $60 million in 1979.\nHow much this figure will increase in the next five years would depend on\nthe development priorities of the Philippines and the ability of the nation\nto borrow long-term capital abroad. Assuming that agricultural lending\n\nNote that the best data available on entire banking system\'s\nlong-term capital loans is dated 1977. Data on the DBP\'s lending\nis more recent, dating to 1979. Therefore, innovative financing schemes by either photovoltaic manufacturers or U.S. and Philippine financial institutions may be\nneeded to promote American photovoltaic systems in the Philippines.\n4.5 Loan Terms for Long-Term Investments\nTo provide some indication of the terms that photovoltaic systems will encounter in receiving long-term loans, this section presents a sample of the\nloan terms for long-term loans currently being given to applicants by longterm financing institutions. Three types of financial institutions were considered: The Development Bank of the Philippines, the Private Development\nCorporation of the Philippines (a private, non-bank financial institution) and\nthe Industrial Guarantee and Loan Fund (a government, non-bank, financial in-\n\nstitution) (see Table 4.5).\n\n4-l0\nJ\n\nc\n\nTARLE\nr.J)NG-TF.RM. These interest rates are substantially lower than\nthose offered by the commercial and savings bank sectors (20t-22t), but\nare substantially higher than rural electrification loans M-30. Maturity\ntime for loans is short---a maximum of 15 years on fixed assets compared\nto 25 years for rural electrification loans.*\n4.6 Conclusion\nThe Philippines has an extensive financial network composed of both private and government owned financial institutions. The majority of investment\nfunds are controlled by the privately owned commercial and savings bank sectors.\nThese funds are available as short-term working capital loans primarily invested in industry, consumption, commerce, real estate and public utilities.\nLong-term investment capital amounts to V6085.2 billion ($832 million). In\n\n1977, of this amount !731.5 million ($100 million) was loaned to the agricultural sector. The Development Bank of the Philippines is the major source\nof long-term capital for agriculture. DHR estimates that only a small part of\nthis capital will be available for financing photovoltaic systems. This lack\nof capital may be a significant barrier tc the marketing of photovoltaic systems.\nConsidering that private venture capital in the Philippines tends to be\nshort-term and loans have high interest rates, there appears td be few alternatives to the development banks as a source of low interest, long-term loans.\n\n* Note that another potential barrier to the financing of P/V systems\nis related to the method used in estimating the costs/benefits of\nP/V and alternatives. These methods are discussed in Section 6.2,\nThe Need for Economic and Financial Analysis.\n\n4-12\n\nAmerican photovoltaic manufacturers or distributors will have to either\nfocus part of their marketing efforts on convincing these institutions\nthat photovoltaics are economically and financially viable and worthy of\nlong-term loans, or introduce innovative financial schemes to promote the\nsales of photovoltaic systems.\n\n0-\n\n1\n\n4-13\nF\n\nS.0 POTENTIAL PHO\' owLTAIC APPLICAnous IN PHILIPPINE AGRICULTURE\nThe major portion of data gathering activities in the Philippines\nwere dedicated to the identification and characterisation of potential\nP/V applications in agriculture. Those applications that were identified\nas possible candidates for P/V power systems were evaluated by:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Level of production/importance of crop in the Philiipine\nagriculture;\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Type of operation and its ability to use a P/V power sources\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Extent of use of operation in the Philippines;\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Extent of current level of mechanization of the operation;\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Size of the power required (less than 25KW capacity).\nThis evaluation process identified a variety of applications such as:\nirrigation of rice, corn and sugarcanes piggeries; rice hulling; corn\nshelling and grinding; and fish refrigeration that represent high P/V use\npotential in the Philippines. The specific agricultural operations identified\nare described in later sections of this chapter and include:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Irrigation\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Rice Drying\n+\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Rice Hulling\t\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Corn Milling\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Continuous Cropping of Rice\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Ice Plant for Fish Preservation\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Fish and Shrimp Hatcheries\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Maintenance Facilities for Large Farms\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Forestry Stations\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Salt Ponds\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Miscellaneous Battery Charging Applications\nThe list of applications characterized covers a wide range of power requirements\n(20W to 12KW capacity), diverse load profiles and varying operating environments.\n5-1\n\nThey also represent a potentially large market for 8/V systems if cost\nand other barriers are overcome. It is wry likely that many other allied\napplications would fall within the operating characteristics described in\nthis chapter.\n5.1 Irrigation\nCurrently, there are 3 million hectares (ha) of rice in cultivation of which\n1.3 million hectares are irrigated by developed water resources. Current\ngovernment policy calls for irrigating the remaining 1.7 million hectares\nat the rate of 100,000 hectares per year. l Other crops requiring irrigation\nare:\ne Vegetables -- These are mainly quarter to half acre plots farmed\nby tenant or owner farmers. The vegetable plots are irrigated\nmanually and require about one to two man-hours per day. Due\nto the subsistence nature of this group of farmers, it is unlikely\nthat they would be a possible P/V system user.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Commercial Crops (sugarcane, pineapple, etc.) -- Generally, these\nare irrigated by means of high volume and/or high pressure irrigation systems. They often use 6-10 inch pumps driven by 50-150hp\ndiesel or electric motors. Thus, it is unlikely that in the nearterm photovoltaic pumping systems could replace these large pumps.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 High Value Cash Crops in the Upland Areas -- These require less water\nper hectare than rice, but a higher pumping head (up to about 50m\nto 60m) is needed. Phgtovoltaic powered pumps could be appropriate\nfor irrigating these crops.\nFarmers participating in large irrigation schemes use small pumps for pumping\nwater from the irrigation channels to the fields. Irrigation water is also\nsupplied by pumps from ground water. The most common pumps size in use is\nthe 4" diameter and smaller discharge, and typical pumping heads range from\n\nlEstuar, Dr. Fiorello, Administrator, National Irrigation Administration.\nThe majority of the irrigation schemes (80%) are and will be gravity fed,\nwhile 10% will employ large pumps of the order of 100-500hp, irrigating\n100-300ha. These irrigation schemes are undertaken by the National Irrigation Administration. The remaining 10% are small irrigation pumping\nschemes Gess than 100ha), falling under the jurisdiction of the Farm\n\nF _\t\n\nSystems Development Corporation.\n\'^\t\n\ne\n\nr\nk\t\n\n5-2\n\nr\n\nSm to SOm, with the average around 15m 2 . Although the average rice field\n\nsize is 2ha, most small pump installations (about 451) service about 6-8\nha, which suggests that some pumps are used by groups of farmers.3\nIn recent years, due to the ever increasing cost of gasoline and diesel fuels, many farmers have drastically reduced the use of small pump\nirrigation. This has resulted in lowered crop productivity and financial\ndistress for the farmers. Thus, a cost-effective source of power independent\nof conventional fuels would be looked upon favorably by not only the farmers\nbut also the rural and development banks who have recently been faced with\nhigh default rates on working capital loans to farmers.\n5.1.1 Irrigation Load Profiles\nCrop irrigation load profiles depend on rainfall. There are four\ndistinct climatic zones in the Philippines. 4 They are:\ne First type:\nApril, the\nregions on\nNegros and\n\nTwo pronounced seasons; one dry from November to\nother wet during the rest of the year. All the\nthe western part of the Islands of Luzon, Mindoro,\nPalawan are of this type.\n\ne Second type: No dry season; with a very pronounced maximum\nrain period from November to January. In this class fall\nCatanduanes, Sorsogon, the eastern part of Albay, the eastern\npart of Leyte and a large portion of eastern Mindanao.\ne Third type: Seasons not very pronounced; relatively dry from\t\nNovember to April and wet drying the rest of the year. The\nmaximum rain periods are not very pronounced with the short\ndry season lasting only from one to three months. Regions\nwith this type of climate are the western part of Cagayan\n(Luzon), Isabela, Nueva Vizcaya, the eastern portion of the\n\n2NIA, unpublished data on small pump characteristics.\n3These refer to pumps installed through NIA financing. Privately purchased\npumps may supply the needs of individual farmers.\nt\n4For more information see: Kintanar, R.L., Annual Climatological Review,\n1974. Philippine Atmosphere, Geophysical and Astronomical Services\nAdministration (PAGASA), March S, 1980.\n\ni\t\n\nF\n\n5-3\n\nf\n\n3\n\nMountain Province, southern Quezon, Masbate, Romblon, northeast\nPanay, eastern Negros, central and southern Cebu, part of\nnorthern Mindanao, and most of eastern Palawan.\nt\n\ne Fourth type: Rainfall more or less evenly distributed throughout the year. The regions affected by this type are the Batanes\nProvince, northeastern Luzon, the southwestern part of the Camerines Norte, the western parts of Camarines Sur and Albay, Sondoc Peninsula, eastern Mindoro, Marinduque, western Leyte, northern Cebu, Bohol and mo*t of central, eastern and southern Mindanao.\nTypical growing seasons for various crops within each of the growing\nseasons can be found in the Philippines Agriculture Factbook and Buyers\nGuide.5\nTable 5.1 shows typical irrigation water requirements for rice and corn\ncultivation in Laguna, which is located in central Luzon, a major rice\ngrowing area in the Philippines. Only double cropping of rice and corn\nare presented in this table.\n5.1.2 Extent of Mechanically Powered Pumping\nSmall pumps, powered by diesel and gasoline engines, are purchased\ndirectly by the farmers. The most likely configuration would be the use\na dryer along with a farm irrigation system.\n5.2.1 Rice Drying Load Profile\nAssume a farmer with two hectares under rice, and 2 MT/ha yield, purchases the dryer. He could use the Model II dryer for four days after harvest to dry his grain. If he double-crops in Luzon or Visayas, he would\nuse it for eight days per year. The total electricity consumption would be\napproximately 35 kwh/harvest, and the power requirements would be about\n1.5 kw (capacity).\n5.2.2 Extent of Mechanically Powered Drying\nTable 5.5 shows the numbers of dryers installed up to 1979. These account\nfor drying only a small portior. of the annual rice production. Sales of\ndryers in 1978 and 1979, amounted to 63 and 73, respectively. 8 Sales in the\nfirst three quarters of 1980, totaled 46. AMMDA estimates that future sales\nof flat type dryers and circulating type dryers to average about 580 and 80 per\nyear for the next five years, respectively. If all rice were to be mechanically\ndried, the number of dryers required for the 1979 rice crop would be 20,0009\nwith a total capacity cf 70,000 MT/day. This can be compared with the current\ncapacity of 10,000 MT/day, or the probable capacity in 1985 of about 22,000 MT/day.\n\n8Source: OMA\n9Assumes 3.5 MT/day drying capacity and 100 crying days/year per dryer.\n\n5-10\n\nf\nb\nr^\n\nTable 5.5\nNUMBER OF RICE DRYERS INSTALLED AS OF 1979\nCapacity MT/day\n(12 Hour O eration^\n\nOwnership\t\n\nNumber of Dryers\t\n\nNational Grain Authority\t\n\n875\t\n\n3,083\n\nPrivate\t\n\n156\t\n\n2,059\n\nIndustry\t\n\n919\t\n\n5,443\n\nTOTAL\t\n\n10,585\n\nSou-.e: National Grain Authority, "National Drying Situation".\n\na\na\nY\n\n\t\n\nS.3 Rice Milling\nOver 99 percent of the 1979 swilling operations are privately owned and\noperated. Two major types of milling machines in use in the Philippines are:\n1\t\n\nj\xc2\xb0\t\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 The Steel Huller -- This is the most popular type of machine\ncurrently in use. Various sizes are available ranging from\n200-300 kg/hour to 600-800 kg/hour models. Power requirements\nrange from 3 hp to 10 hp (electric) 10 . They account for over\n95\xe2\x80\xa2 of rice mills installed, but only 57\xe2\x80\xa2 of the rice-milling\ncapacity.11\n\xe2\x80\xa2 The Cone Type Mills -- These account for less than 5% of the rice\nmills installed, but swill about 43.* of the rice. The average\ncapacity of a mill is about 600 kq/hour and the power requirements range from 10 hp to 40 hp (electric).11\nGenerally, the mills are powered by diesel engines. A recent study12\nfound that an average of 11 hp is required tc% drive steel huller mills,\nwhile cone type mills average 42 hp. Cone type mills are generally located\nnear the main throughfares and in towns away from the main source of paddy.\nin contrast, steel huller mills are found in rural areas near the main\nsource of paddy. This mill type is often household oriented, serving primarily the needs of consumers living within the village.\n5.3.1 Rice Milling Load Profile\nRice mills are operated continuously throughout the year. However, the\nintensity of use varies from month to month, with peaks coinciding with\nthe planting months.\nA recent study 12 showed that the steel hullers were utilized an average\n\n10 Source: Polygon Agro-Industrial Corporation, Manila, Philippines.\nt\t\n\n"\'national Grain Authority, "National Milling Situation", unpublished typescript.\n\na\t\ni\t\n\n12"The Technical and Economic Characteristics of Rice Post-production\nSystems in the Bicol River Basin."
