Thus, an estimate of the total\nnumber of corn grinders in use in 1979 is about 2700 and the number of\nroller mills is about 700.16\nAMMDA expects sales of corn grinders to average around 550 per year for\nthe next five years. The sales of corn roller mills are expected to average\nabout 60 per year for the next five years.\n5.5 Continuous Cropping of Rice\n\n\t\nM.\n\nContinuous cropping of rice ("rice garden") is a new rice production systam currently under investigation by the International Rice Research Institute (IRRI) in Los Banos, Philippines. Presently, it 1-as not been introduceC\nas a general agricultural practice. It has certain energy use chrracteristics\nthat make it ideally suited for P/V systems use. In this technique, rice\nis continuously cultivated and harvested, resulting in four harvests per year.\nIRRI may soon introduce this technique to the farming community in the Philippines\n\n16 Assumed80% of the corn is machine ground; 422,400 kg of corn is ground\nby one grinder in a year (i.e., 200 kg/hour x 8 hours/day x 22 days/month\nx 12 months/year); 2,073,600 kg of corn is grounded by one roller mill in\na year (1200 kg/hour x 6 hours/day x 24 days/month x 12 months/year); and\n80% of the mills are the "grinder" type.\n\n5-16\n\nr ^-\n\nF\t\n\nTABLE 5.8\nCORN MILLING OPERATING CHARACTERISTICS\n\nHours/Day\n\nCorresponding\nDays/Month\n\t\n\nLow\t\n\nAverage \t\n\nHigh\n\nGrinder Mills\t\n\n3\t\n\n8\t\n\n14\n\nRoller Mills\t\n\n3\t\n\n6\t\n\n13\t\n\n\t\n\n\t\n\nLow\t\n\nAverage\t\n\nHigh\n\n16\t\n\n22\t\n\n23\n\n20\t\n\n24\t\n\n28\n\nSource: Calculated from data in Almeda, et.al ., op. cit.\n\nx\nt\n\n\t\t\n\nand other rice growing countries around the world, with the hope that it\nt\t\n\ncould provide an effective moans in increasing worldwide rice production.\n\ni\nFor a typical rice garden of about 1 hectare, the land is divided into\n13 rice paddies of approximately 800 m 3 , and four seed beds of 25 si t each.\nEach plot is sequentially cultivated, planted, and harvested in a weekly\nschedule. The principle behind the sequential operation is that by the\ntime the 13th plot is cultivated, the 1st plot is ready for harvest and re-\n\nV ,\t\n\nplanting. Thus, four crops/year are possible per hectare compared to the\ncurrent Philippine average of about 1.1 crops/year/hectare.\nThe advantages of the rice garden technique are:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Seasonal labor peaks are dispersed into constant year-round\nlabor use that generate employment opportunities throughout\nthe years\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Risk of insect attack or weather damage is minimized because\nrice in each plot is in a different stage of growths and\n\xe2\x80\xa2 The continuous flow of cash from weekly sales allows the farmer\nto finance the farm operation himself and eliminates the need\nfor financing.\nOn the other hand, the continuous production system has rather stringent\nrequirements for successful implementation, such as:\ne Control over a dependable year-round water supply is essential.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Rice in all stages of growth in a small area, and the continued\nuse of insecticides, may bring about insect resistance to insecticides quicker than in other less intensive systems.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 A\n\nhigh degree of management ability by the farmer is needed.\n\nRice farmers may also face resistance from laborers or neighbors if they\ntry to modify their rice farming to use the continuous system.\n\t\ni\n\nTypical equipment needed for a successful rice garden operation\nincludes: 6.5 hp tiller, 5-7 hp small thresher, small grain dryer, aad\nan irrigation pump. The latter is needed it a dependable year-rounu,\n\na\n5-18\n\ngravity fed water supply is unavailable. 17\n5.5.1 Rice Garden Load Profiles\nThe compatibility of the rice garden method with P/V power systems is due\nto the need for a year-round supply of irrigation water which closely matches\nthe solar insolation levels which are greatest during the dry months. Table 5.9\nshows the monthly water requirements for a 1 hectare rice garden. Dater requirements are the most important parameter defining monthly energy requirements.\n5.5.2 Extent of Use of Rice Gardens\nCurrently, the rice garden method is an experimental technique. It is\nabout to be publicized worldwide by IRRI. If the worldwide rice growing community\nbegins to accept this new technique, then the identification of P/V with rice\ngarden method could result in the opening-up of a large marxet for P/V powered\nirrigation systems.\n5.6 Ice Plant\nThe small ice plant is an innovative technology developed by Schultz\nIndustries with the technical assistance of the Technology Utilization\nSupport System (TUSS) of the Technology Resource Center (TRC) in the Philippines. It is one of several improved technologies being promoted by the\nTRC for commercial utilization. The ice plant answers the need of countryside farmers and fishermen for a reliable source of ice and refrigeration.\nIt is ideal for rural areas and fishing villages too small or too remote to\nbe serviced by large ice plants. In such villages, ice has become a very\nexpensive and scarce commodity.\n\n27 Formore information see:\nMorooka, Y., Herdt, R.W. and Haws, L.D., "An Analysis of the Labor-Intensive Continuous Rice Production System at IRRI." The freezing tank can be used as cold\nstorage by simply removing the brine solution. And its condensing fan can\ndry crops, fish and clothes.\nThe ice plant has many advantages over ordinary ice plants, such as:\ne low cost\t\n\nj\n\ne ease-of-operation (requires only two people\nto run it)\ne capacity sufficient for the needs of small\ncommunities\ne reduced delivery costs.\n5.6.1 Ice plant profile\nThe ice plant is compact and self-contained. Running on a two-cylinder\ndiesel engine, it can produce 1.5 tons of block ice a day, with three cycles\nper 24-hour operation. Maximum processing time is eight hours requiring\none cubic meter of fresh potable water per Say. The ice plant requires\nmirimun technical skills to operate and needs only two operators.\nDue to the 24-hour cperating characteristics of the plant, the size\nof the P/V array required would be large: however, this example is used\nto demonstrate the cost characteristics of a system at the ulper end of\nthe scale of technologies being investigated in this study. Technical\nSpecification of the system is as follows: The main component is an ice\nmaker consisting of an open-type compressor, twin cylinder engine, fly\nwheel and drive pulley, refrigerant freon - 12, service valves, receiver\nand oil separator. The technical features of the 1.5 ton ice plant include:\nt\n\ni\n5-21\nD\n{\n\n1\n\ni\ne 29-21 hp diesel engine or 15 hp electric motor\ne 2.25 liters/hour of diesel cons=qption\ns open-type compressor with 7-10 tons refrigarating capacity\n5.6.2\n\nExtent of Use of Icy: Plant\n\nIt is estimated that a plant of this size would be adequate for a\nfishing village with a daily fish catch of 1.5 MT/day or a village with\na population of 15,000.\nCurrently, there are very many fishing villages too far from large\nice plants that have to limit their fish harvest due to lack of preservation facilities. These fishermen, (600,000 in number), account for 50%\nof the fish harvest.\n\nis\n\nSince the average annual fish harvest of such\n\nfishermen is 400 kg/year/fishermen, 19 the quantity of ice required for preservation is 240 million kg/year. Thus, the potential number of ice plants\nrequired would be at least 400.\n5.7 Prawn and Fish Hatchery\nPrawn and fish aquaculture in brackish and fresh water ponds is an\nindustry that is rapidly being developed in the Philippines. They are developed from mangrove swamps, estuarine\nl\nareas, tidal flats, and shallow coastal areas, and are used mainly for the\nrearing of bangus (milkfish) and shrimp, in brackish water. \t\nt\n\nThe average size of a fish pond operation is 20 ha. Currently, fish\nponds operate at low stocking densities of less than 10,000 fry/ha (compared\nto 200,000 fry/ha in Taiwan), and use natural algae for feed. There\nis a growing trend, and a government policy 35 for increasing the yield and\noutput from fish pond operations. Currently, over 95% of the ponds depend\non tidal water exchange for water transfer. However, with increased fish\nstocking densities, water pumping will be essential since a water exchange\nrate of 20% will be required (20,000 gal/hr/ha -- 75 a3/hr/ha).\n5.9.1 Fish Pond Load Profi le\nPumps are operated throughout the year. Pumping takes place between\n0400 and 0900 hours for about 5 hours. Frequency of operation is 2-3\ntimes a week. During the hotter months (March to June in the Iloilo area)\nan additional 2-3 hours of pumping is required at about 1500 hours.\nType of pumps required for fish pond water exchange are low head (2-3 m),\nhigh volume pumps. The quantity of salt produced annually\namounts to about 200,000 MT\n\n27\n\n, produced on about 200-250 salt farms. 29\n\nThe process employed in salt manufacturing is rather simples sea\nwater is pumped into retention tanks and as its salt concentration increases,\nthrough evaporation the liquor is transferred to smaller tanks. The latter\ntransfers are by gravity. Finally, the highly concentrated liquor is transferred to brick-lined crystallation tanks. Pumping is sometimes required at this\nstage, depending on the salt pond design. When all the water has evaporated,\nthe salt is manually scraped off the bricks and stored in a silo or sacks.\nSalt is manufactured only during the dry season, which in Iloilo, a\nmajor salt producing region, is January to May. During the wet season,\nthe salt ponds are used to cultivate backish water, such as ban s (milkfish) or prawns. An average size of a salt manufacturing facility is about\n10 ha. Currently no pumping is required for fish cultivation.\n26\n\nsureau of Fisheries and Aquatic Resources.\n\n27 PhilippineYearbook, 1979.\n28\nDHR estimate based on average production per farm of about 600-800 MT/ha.\n\n5-29\n\n...^.^..*n . - .off-\n\n.-.r^\xc2\xb0+^ " ,^.^- . - r - ^\xe2\x80\xa2..,._r ^. *+r\n\nTABLE 5.13\n_POWER REQUIRED AND USAGE PROFILE FOR\nFISH POND OPERATION (20 HA)\n\nEquipment\t\n\nPower\t\n\nUsage Profile\n\nFour 6" axial flow pumps 5 hp gasoline \t\n\nOR\t\n\n5 hours / day (0400-0900 hours),\n3 days /week, 52 weeks/year\nAND\n\nFour 4" centrifugal pumps 10 hp diesel \t\n\nSource: DHR site visits\n\n5-30\n\n2 hours / day (1500 - 1700 hours),\n3 days /week during March to June\n\n\t\n\n,f\t\ni\n\nHowever, as mentioned previously, if stocking densities are to be increased,\nmechanical pumping will be required.\n5.10.1 Salt Pond Load Profile\n\n\t\n0\n\nA typical pumping unit required for salt manufacturing is a 6" low\nhead, high volume centrifugal pump coupled to a 10 to 15-hp diesel engine.\nThis is used to pump sea water into the first concentrating tank. These includes\ne Fisheries stock gathering -- Most hatcheries must collect young\nfry or specimen from the wild or ocean and then transfer thgm to\nthe hatchery. During the collection process, which is done in\nremo.e locations, power is required for pumping small amounts of\nwatear or for aeration. Currently these fractional horsepower\npumps are powered using small gasoline engines or batteries.\nProblems with the current power sources are: noisy gasoline\nengines disturb fish collection: and batteries have to be taken\ninto town for charging, resulting in significant damage to the\nbatteries due to mishandling.\ne Communication -- Most large farms and fish pond operations require\npower to operate a transceiver. Currrently these are battery powered, with the inherent problems associated with battery charging.\n5-31\n\n\t\n\nTABLE 5.14\nPOWER REQUIRED AND USAGE PROFILE FOR SALT/FISH PRODUCTION\nUsage Profile\nMay June - Decembwr\nJanua\t\n\xe2\x80\x94\n(salt)\t\ns\t\n\nEquipment\n\nPower Required\n\nFuel Consumed\n\n6 " pump\n\n10-15 hp\n\n10 liters/day\n\n5 hours/day\t\n\n5 hours/day\n\nTwo 3" pumps\n\n6-8 hp\n\n10 liters/3 days\nfor two pumps\n\n1 hour/day\t\n\n---\n\nSource: Iloilo salt farm site visits.\n\nt\n\n5-32\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 battery charging in logging sites -- At pr\xe2\x80\xa2senL considerable inconvenience is caused to logging operations in forests due to\ndischarged batteries. At this point the market share of P/V\nwill be close to zero as the conventional systems have the advantages of\nexisting supply and repair systems, tradition, less initial capital investment and greater end-use flexibility. As the cost advantage of P/V\nover conventional systems grows so will its market share. Once the cost\nequality point for P/V is passed, their rate of penetration will be determined by other factors such as: equipment turnaround rates, awareness,\navailability of finance, marketing strategies, availability of distribution,\ninstallation, and maintenance facilities and other market related factors.\nAnother assumption made in estimating the market is that P/V systems will\nfirst penetrate into areas where users are already using mechanically or\nelectrically powered equipment. For example, P/V systems will be more\nacceptable to a farmer using a gasoline engine driven pump than one who\nirrigates the field manually.\nIn Section 6.2, the methodology used in the market size estimation\nanalysis is described briefly. Section 6.3 discusses the necessity of\nconducting an economic cost/benefit as well as financial cost/benefit\nanalysis. Section 6.4 awmarizes the market and institutional factors\nthat constitute incentives and barriers to P/V use. In Section 6.5, a\nseries of cost-competitive analyses are described. It identifies the year\nin\n\nwhich various P/V applications become equal in cost (on a life-cycle\n\nbasis) to the least expensive conventional power system. Finally, in\n\n6-1\n\nSection 6.6, an annual potential market size estimate is obtained for\n1982 through 1990.\n6.2 Market Size Estimation Methodology\nMarket size estimates are developed in two stages. In Stage 1, the\nlife-cycle cost of P/V systems is compared to its nearest conventional\npower system for a variety of applications. For example, for applications\nrequiring a power capacity of about 1 KW or less, the competitor is assumed\nto be gasoline driven generators. For larger systems, the competitor is\ndiesel driven generators. The life-cycle costs of P/V systems and the\ncompeting conventional systems are calculated on an annual system purchase\nbasis, from 1980 to 1990. Since the P/V array and balance of system costs\nare steadily decreasing and conventional system and fuel costs are increasing, at some point in time the costs became equal. That is to say, when\ncosts became equal, a P/V system installed in that year costs as much as\na conventional system installed in the same year, as measured on a lifecycle basis. Thus, the analysis enables one to determine the year when\nP/V systems first became competitive with the competing conventional systems.\nIn the next stage, the upper bound on the P/V market (defined by the extent\nof use of various applications) is modified by the equipment turnaround\nrates, financing availability and other factors to obtain an estimate of\nthe possible annual sales of P/V systems in the Philippines. Due to the\ninherent uncertainties prevalent in such predictions, we provide three market\nsize estimates: "most likely", "increased activity" and "optimistic", and\ntheir definitions are described later.\n6.2.1 Cost Analysis\nThe cost analyses are based on accepted life-cycle costing principles.\nBoth a financial and an economic life-cycle cost analysis are conducted\ndue to the reasons explained in Section 6.3.\n6-2\n\nTo reflect inherent uncertainties prevalent in predicting future\ntrends, several scenarios are considered.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Financial analysis using a real fuel cost escalation of 16% per\nyear for gasoline and 10 \xe2\x99\xa6 per year for diesel. This corresponds\nto historical escalation rates from 1975 to 1980. 1 For each case,\ntwo loan rates of 21% and 14% are used. The former corresponds\nto the current cowercial bank rate and the latter to the development bank loan rate.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Financial analysis using a real fuel cost escalation of 3\xe2\x80\xa2 per\nyear for gasoline and diesel and a loan rate of 14%.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Economic analysis using a real discount rate of 12\xe2\x80\xa2 and a real\nfuel cost escalation rate of 3s per year. These rates were\nrecommended for use by Dr. Mohan Munasinghe, Energy Economist,\nThe World Bank, Washington, D.C.\nThe methodology used for the analyses and the input data assumption are\ndescribed in Appendex E.\n6.2.2 Market Size Estimation\nP/V market size estimation methodologies that use current and projected\ngasoline and diesel generator sales as an indicator of possible P/V sales can be\nmisleading. For example, in the Philippines most large diesel driven generators\nare used to provide back-up power in the case of grid power failure. Furthermore,\nin many agricultural applications, gasoline and diesel engines are directly\ncoupled to equipment, without the need for an electricity generator. Thus,\nin this study, generator sales are not an adequate indicator since they do not\ntake into account a large segment of the feasible agricultural sector market.\nOur "bottom-up" approach begi,.s by estimating the upper-bound of the\npotential market. The upper-bound is defined as the peak power required for\nthe total number of cost competitive, practical applications. The upper-bound\nis modified by the new equipment installation rate to obtain an estimate of\n\n1 This assumption implies that the extraordinary fuel cost escalation from\n1975 to 1980 will continue. At 10 \xe2\x99\xa6 and 16% real fuel cost escalation, a\nbarrel costing $40 in 1980 will cost in 1990 (in constant dollars) $104\nand $176 respectively.\n6-3\n\n6\n\nthe applications that may be installed in a particular year. The modified\npotential market is then further reduced to reflect other constraints to\nc\n\nachieving the potential market. These include awareness * financing, institutional barriers, and other market related factors.\n6.3 Need for Financial and Economic Cost/Benefit Analysis\n\n^a\nLong-term financing of photovoltaic systems is likely to be handled\nthrough the government financial system. Host long-term financing in agriculture is directed through the development banks, which are in turn partially\nfinanced through multi-lateral donor agencies, such as the Asian Development\nBank and the world Bank and bilateral agreements between the Philippines\nand donor countries. These banks function not only as lending agencies but\nalso as agents of government policy objectives. This dual function creates\nthe need within the development banks to evaluate loans in terms of their\neconomic benefits to the nation as well as their benefits to the potential\napplicant. The World Bank has been instrumental in developing a methodology\nfor the evaluation of economic benefits to the nation from investment projects. Most development banks financed by the world Bank have been encouraged\nto adopt this economic analysis of projects as a part of their lending evaluations in addition to the more traditional, business-oriented, financial\nanalysis.2\nInterviews with the Development Bank of the Philippines\' planners indicated that the bank will, in the future, adopt the economic analysis of\nprojects as part of their evaluation of loans. Once this practice has been\ninstituted, loans will be given according to the following criteria:\ne Loans would be given if projects pass both and economic and financial analysis.\n\ndetails on the World Bank economic analysis methodology soot Lyn\nire and Herman G. Van Der Tak, Economic Analysis of Projects, the\nns Hopkins University Press, 1975.\n6-4\n\ni\n\ni\n\ne e If a project passes an economic analysis and not a financial\nanalysis, it would be sore likely to receive funding than if\nit passes a financial analysis and tails an economic analysis.\ni Finally, if a project fails both an economic analysis and\nfinancial analysis it will not receive funding.\nConsidering that most long-term agricultural financing in the\nPhilippines is presently directed through the Development Bank of the\nPhilippines, American photovoltaic firms wishing to sell in the future\nto the government or to government-financed projects may have to justify\non economic grounds the viability of these systems. DHR, in considering\npotential photovoltaic applications, has included in its evaluation of\nphotovoltaic systems both an economic and a financial analysis. The\npurpose of using both types of economic evaluation is to provide photovoltaic manufacturers with an idea of the economics under which these\nsystems will be financed by the Development Bank of the Philippines.\nBriefly, an economic analysis is defined here as an attempt to value\nthe contribution to the country\'s basic socioeconomic objectives made by\na project. In practice, an economic analysis involves the use of prices\nfor goods and services that are free from distortions in the marketplace\nand reflect the real, economic value of the good. For example, price of\ngasoline at the National Philippine Oil Corporation refinery cutlet is ($1.18\nper gallon, including wholesale markup). Taxes and duties have raised this\nprice to ($2.42 per gallon) and have distorted the market for gasoline.3\nIn an economic analysis, the price of $1.18 per gallon would be used in the\nevaluation of the costs/benefits of a gasoline engine. This approach differs\nfrom a financial analysis that would use the higher price inclusive of taxes\n\n3 The prices are as of August 15, 1980. The corresponding diesel prices\nare as follows: Refinery Outlet: $1.18 per gallon (including wholesale\nmarkup)i Retails $1.43 per gallon.\n\n6-S\n\nand duties. To a businessman, the latter approach is much sore valuable\nthan the forcer. However, to a development planner the economic analysis\nwould be relatively more important. DHR\'s analysis is not intended to be\na primer on economic analysis of projects. It is directed at illustrating\nthe potential changes that could occur in the market for photovoltaics as\na result of using a different methodology for economic evaluation. In\ngeneral, it can be said that the economic analysis of projects will tend to delay\nintroduction of photovoltaic systems into the marketjlace. Further details\nwill be provided on the factors that influence the economic analysis of\nphotovoltaics in the next section.\n6.4 Summary of Incentives and Barriers to P/V Use in Agriculture\nThe pur pose of this section is to summarize DHR\'s perceptions as to the\nmajor non-economic incentives and constraints to P/V use in Philippines agriculture. The Philippines offers a mixed bag of incentives and disincentives\nfor the use of photovoltaics in agriculture. On one hand, the government\noffers a number of financial incentives designed to spur the introduction\nand use of non-conventional energy technologies (including P/V) into Philippine society, while on the other hand, there are a variety of business and\nfinancial practices that will act as constraints on the development of a\nP/V market. For American P/V manufacturers, these constraints center on:\n1) the lack of long-term capital in the agricultural sector that could be used\nto finance P/V products; 2) the possibility of foreign competition particularly from\nGerman, Japanese and i\'rench P/V manufacturers; 3) potential service/maintenance problems for BOS components that require skilled labor or storage of\nhigh value parts; 4) existing credit arrangements between dealers/wholesalers/\nmanufacturers; and finally 5) the degree of awareness and lack of information\non behalf of end-users and skepticism by planners and policy makers about P/V.\n\n=y\t\n\n6-6\n;\n. t\n\n:\t\n\n4\n\n4\n\nA\t\n\ni\nt\n\nA a wary of current Philippine business/financial practices is provided in Table 6.1 with DHR\'a perception of the relative degree of constraint/\n3\nincentive for each of the practices identified. Further detail on each\nk\t\n\nconstraint/incentive can be found within the text.\nIn developing a market for photovoltaic& in the Philippines, the constraints, particularly the lack of long-term capital, will outweigh the incentives given for photovoltaic use. The high initial capital costs for\nphotovoltaic systems coupled with the shortage of long-term loans would\nprevent many consumers who would be interested in P/V systems from purchasing\nthese systems. DHR feels that the present inadequacy of long-term capital\nin the Philippines is the single most important impediment to the development\nof the P/V market.\n6.5 Cost Analyses\nThe financial and economic cost analyses are based on methods, assumptions and data currently being used by the Filipino financial community in\n\ni\nassessing the feasibility of agriculture sector capital investments. The\ne\ninput to the analyses are described in detail in Appendix E. Also described\nin the same appendix is the power required and energy usage profile for the\napplications described in the previous chapter.\nTable 6.2 shows the results of the financial analyses real fuel escalation\nrates of 16% (gasoline) and 10% (diesel) in two cases: commercial bank loan\nrates of 21% and development bank loan rate of 14%. The table indicates the peak array size necessary to supply the energy required for varions applications, the first year in which the P/V system becomes equal\nin cost with its closest competitor, and the life-cycle cost per Kwh in\nthe first year of cost-competitiveness (in nominal dollars). Financing\nwould be possible and encouraged if, as required by Presidental Decree, DS\'\nhas to give additional incantives to renewable energy systems. They were:\nAwareness -- There is, in general, a lack of awareness among endusers, private and public banking communities, and government\ndecision-makers as to the possibilities for P/V use.\ne Cost-Competitiveness -- P/V systems must be cost-competitive with\nthe least cost, practical alternative, on a life-cycle basis before\nP/V use will be seriously contemplated.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Financial -- The Philippines is faced with a serious capital\nconstraint. Consequently, only a few institutions currently\nmake long-term loans to the agricultural sector.\n6.6.1 Scenario Definition\n\xe2\x80\xa2\nOur approach to estimating the market size evaluates each of the above\ncriteria and provides three estimates (status quo, increased activity and optimistic); namely, number of cost-competitive applications or finance, based\non the tightest constraint.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 "Status No" scenario assumes that the current status quo is maintained\nand fuel costs have a low escalation rate. That is, the barriers and incentives summarized in Section 6.4, continue to be relevant and applicable in\nthe future.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 "Increased Activity" scenario assumes greater government interest,\nadditional government financing, aggressive marketing by P/V companies, considerable end-user interest and awareness, low (3%) real fuel escalation\nrates, and self-financing by larger operations in addition to the incentives\nassumed in previous cases.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 "Optimistic" scenario assumes a definite government commitment\ntowards P/V, priority government financing, very strong end-user interest,\nsmaller operations entering the market, the private financial community funding for P/V systems and historical fuel cost escalation rates, in addition to\nthe assumptions described in the previous scenario.\n6-18\n\nf\nr\n\nIn Section 6.6.2, market estimates based on cost-competitiveness are\nmade assuming no shortage of capital. In Section 6.6.3, market estimates\nbased on capital constraints are made. The set of market size estimates that\nare more constrained will be used as the DHR\'s best estimate for P/V use in\nPhilippines agriculture.\n6.6.2 Market Estimate Based on Cost-Competitiveness\nTable 6.4 shows the upper bound on P/V power requirements based on the\nfinancial and economic analysis described in Section 6.4. The power requirements were calculated by multiplying the number of cost-competitive\napplications by the peak power requirements per unit. It shows the demand\nfor P/V power in MWp if all cost-competitive applications began using P/V\nsystems. While this table indicates an upper-bound, it cannot be used to\ndetermine the number of possible P/V conversions in a given year since a\ntotal one-time conversion is impractical.\nA better indicator is to assume that equipment being installed in a\nparticular year could be considered for P/V conversion. An indication of\nannual conversion rates may be obtained by examining the actual number of\nagricultural equipment in use and the expected annual sales as projected\nby AMMDA. In most cases, equipment installed per year (equipment turn-over\nplus new installations) amounts to about 10% of the existing stock. For\nthe purposes of estimating a P/V market share, we assume that 10% of equipment installed will use P/V. These assumptions will be imposed on each\nscenario.\nBased on the scenario definitions, the economic analysis market penetration corresponds most closely to the "status quo" case. The financial\nanalysis case with a 14% loan rate and 3% fuel cost escalation corresponds\nto the "increased activity" scenario. Capital availability estimates are\nshorn in Table 6.6. The estimates reflect the fact that there is a significant\n"learning" or awareness problem associate3 with P/V use and until that barrier\nis broken, no significant amounts of P/V systems will be installed. DHR\nestimates that it will be at least 1985 before this barrier is minimized.\nOn the more positive side, it is assumed that most small applications,\ncurrently cost-competitive, will be purchased by the more affluent users.\nThese include transceivers, logging operation battery chargers, maintenance\nyard power supply, forestry stations, and prawn hatcheries. In most cases,\nthe amount of capital required is well within the resources of the users (for\nexample, annual revenue from a prawn hatchery is about $60,000). The small\napplications used by the more affluent groups amount to about S.5 MWp of power\nfor the financial analyses cases and about 1 MWp for the economic analysis\ncase. The data in Table 6.6 reflects only financing available from Filipino\nsources for commercial use of P/V and does not take into account foreign funds\nsupplied for demonstration projects. Estimation of funds available for the\nlatter activities is fraught with even greater uncertainties. Thus, making\nsuch estimates would be fruitless. Table 6.7 shows quantity of P/V systems\nthat can be installed for financing levels in Table 6.6. This is based on\nP/V system costa shown in Table 3 in Appendix E.\n\n1 The total 1977 long-term financing in the Philippine agriculture sector\nwas approximately $60 million.\n\n6-21\n\nTABLE 6.5\nMARKET SIZE ESTIMATIONS BASED ON COST - Thus, devising innova-\n\ntive financing schemes and promotional campaigns could have a wry high payoff. The market would be mainly small power applications in the pre-1985\nperiod with sales to affluent individuals or corporations. In the post-1985\nperiod, MIR speculates that the market for larger systems would open up with\nlong-term financing provided by the Philippine banking community.\nAs a cOMWison of Table 6.5 and 6.7 clearly shows, capital availability\nwould be the most constraining factor determining market size. Thus, the\nmarket estimates in Table 6.7 would be representative of what can be expected\nin terms of P/V system sales in the Philippine agriculture sector.\nAdditionally, there are many characteristics of the Philippine agricultural and energy sectors that will influence the market for P/V systems.\nAmong the most favorable factors are:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Stated government policy towards promotion of renewable, and\ndispersed power sources.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Financial incentives for the production and use of P/V systems.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 High cost of conventional energy supplies such as gasoline and\ndiesel.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Entrepreneurial interest in P/V systems.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Cost-competitiveness of P/V systems when coWared to gasoline and\ndiesel power sources in a wide range of agricultural applications.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Availability of suitable marketing channels for sale of P/V\nsystems in the agricultural sector.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 The shortage of skilled labor for maintaining ca geting gasoline\nand diesel powered systems.\n6-zs\n\n\t\n\nHowever, very significant and serious barriers prevent achieving the technically feasible, cost-competitive market for P/V systems in the agricultural\nsector. These includes The lack of awareness of the possibilities for P/V\nsystemst high first costs of P/V when c=Wared to competing systems; and,\nthe inadequacy of long term capital for financing P/V systems.\n\nv\n\nI\t\n\nt\nL\n\ne\n\n6-26\na\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n7.0 DUSIVE" ENVIRONMENT\n7\xe2\x80\x9e1 Level of Public Awareness\nThe OHR team estimated that only about 20 percent of the individuals\ncontacted and interviewed had any substantial knowledge about P/V systems.\nHowever, within the agricultural sector and related organizations or\nagencies contacted by the team, the level crf awareness was higher, as\nalmost all organizations or agencies had one or more persons who were aware\nof what P/V systems were and how they can generally be utilized for power.\nA higher level of awareness and sophistication about P/V systems was found\nin several key agencies outside the Ministry of Energy. For exaWles in\nthe Ministry of Agriculture, the National Grains Authority, and the National\nirrigation Administration people had bcen considering possible applications\nand haj, therefore, educated themselves about P/V systems. However, in most\ninstances where there was such a high level of awareness, the people were\nprofessionals who were actively involved in trying to solve energy problems\nin the agriculture, fisheries, or forestry sectors. \t\nt\nIn summary, the OHR team found that although general awareness was\nlow, that in almost all agencies or organizations it was not difficult to\nfind individuals who had some general knowledge of P/V systems.\nAs a result of extensive meetings with all of the major gown-anent\nagencies related to agriculture (and, therefore having important relationships\nto the future of P/V systems in the Philippines -- see Appendix A), the aHR\nteam felt that there is certainly a strong interest in P/V systems among\nthese government agencies. Even in those instances where awareness was not\nhigh, a simple P/V demonstration or explanation by the OHR team usually resalted in a strong interest to explore P/V possibilities. However, there is\nnot yet a concentrated or strong effort to adopt or utilize P/V systems.\nPresent interest is mainly confined to the demonstration level.\n7-1\n\nE\n\nA number of officials were able to identify initial application where\nP/V systems could be employed---for example, in agency communication systems\nor in field extension operations related to research and development activities. Int"est in research and testing of various applications for example,\nin water pumping, powering fans for dryers, or multiple-use applications.\nInterest also exists for uses such as charging remote batteries, powering\nremote communication equipment, or running media equipment.\nIn moving beyuiv" expurimentation and demonstration, people, especially\nthose involved in energy work within agencies, become skeptical as to the\nappropriateness or match of P/V to their needs. It was repeatedly mentioned\nto the DHR team that their major concern or reservation was based on the high\npresent market cost of P/V systems. This seems to be the key inhibitor to\na more widespread and higher level of interest necessary to move beyond demonstrations. People in the Philippines are taking a wait-and-see attitude\ntoward i\'/V. An improvement in the relative cost of P/V to competing energy\nsources will address this\n\ninhibiting factor,\n\nand create an atmosphere and\n\npossibility of increased interest. At that time, it will be possible to\naddress remaining concerns about P/V performance (e.g., reliability and durability) that can\n\nonly cane\n\nwith user experience\n\nin the\n\nfield.\n\n7.2 Entrepreneurial Interest\nThere is currently an active and enthusiastic entrepreneurial interest\nin photovoltaics by Filipino businessmen. In interviews\n\nwiW both\n\nlarge\n\nand small industrialists, there was the attitude that photovoltaics could\ndevelop as a viable energy industry in the Philippines. Both a large industrial corporation and a large commercial bank have expressed interest\n\n`\t\n\nTo protect the confidentiality of DHR sources, this section presents a\ngeneral overview of DHR impressions of Philippine entrepreneurial interest\nin photovoltaics.\n\nr\n7-2\n\n10f\nr\n\nin financing economically viable photovoltaic projects, as well as establishing a local photovoltaic industry. Smaller firms, presently involved in\nselling flat plate solar collectors, have also expressed an interest in\nestablishing contact with American photovoltaic manufacturers with the purposes\nof either establishing joint manufacturing ventures or marketing of photovoltaic systems.\nIn general, the level of knowledge and sophistication about potential\napplications, markets and photovoltaic systems is high in larger firms. In\naddition, larger firms appeared to be interested in technology transfer\narrangements with the United States, while smaller entrepreneurs expressed\na willingness to act as dealers for photvoltaic systems. A number of entrepreneurs, both large and small, inquired about the possibility of setting up\na regional production facility in the Philippines to service the ASEAN marlet (Singapore, Thailand, Indonesia, Malaysia and the Philippines). These\nentrepreneurs felt that\n\nit may be possible to obtain preferential tariff\n\nduties on an ASEAN photovoltaic facility from the ASEAN community. One firm\nhas advertised small photovoltaic systems in Philippine newspapers. These\nsystems were designed to power radios and electric fans and geared to the\nupper-middle and upper class market. To date this businessmen has not\nbeen able to penetrate this market successfully. One problem (often\ncited by businessmen) is the fact that potential customers are familiar with\nmuch larger systems. For example, farmers irrigating their fields commonly\nuse 4"-6" pumps coupled to 5 to 10 hp gasoline or diesel engines. Smaller\nphotovoltaic systems (below lXW) are viewed with sketpicism by farmers who\nfeel that P/V cannot irrigate as large an area as a gasoline or a diesel\n\n7-3\n\nFinally, there have been a number of foreign and American firms that\ni.\nhave sold photovoltaic systems to the Philippines Hon-Conventional Energy\nCenter and the Philippines Armed Forces for demonstration and testing purposes. Satisfaction with these systems sold to the government and private\nsectors appeared to be high.\n7.3 Overview of Philippine Business Practices in the Electric Generation\nand Transmission &iuipment Areas\nThe bulk of electric generating and transmission equipment sold in\nthe Philippines flows either directly from manufacturers to end-users or\nfrom manufacturers through importer/dealers to end-users. There is also\nwholesale/retail distribution of small electrical items such as switches\nand low-voltage wire, but this distribution constitutes a small portion of\ntotal market value.\nIn the case of imported items, importer/dealers most often act as\nexclusive agents for overseas suppliers and promote the products to endusers. In some cases the importer/dealers will keep stock of items but in\nmost instances, particularly for high-value items, sales are on an indent\nbasis: the importer/dealer makes payment to the overseas supplier and then\ncollects from the customer, or the customer opens a letter of credit, or\nsome other form of payment to the overseas supplier with the overseas\nsupplier reserving a commission for the local importer/dealer. Foreigners\ncannot own retail operations in the Philippines; but can wholesale their\nproducts.\nCredit terms are normally up to 60 days for small equipment items while\nfor large items installment payment terms are often arranged which include\n\n4\n\nNote that a substantial portion of this information has teen taken from\n"Energy Systems Equipment Market Research in the Philippines." Mobius\nResearch, May 1979,and verified through interviews by the DHR project staff.\n\ne\n7-4\n\nto=ms of 25% deposit with the remainder payable in monthly installsients\nover a 2-year period.\nWarranty terms normally range between one and two years. For example,\nin the case of diesel generating sets, typical warranty torsos would be two\nyears for standby units and one year for primary power use. Such a\nwarranty would include parts and labor for repairs arising from equipment\nfaults.\nService is a very important factor in sales of equipment in the\nPhilippines, particularly service and spare parts facilities outside the\nGreater Manila Area. End-users favor dealers who have a plentiful and easily\navailable supply of spare parts in addition to availability of workers\ncapable of undertaking the repairs. For smaller generating systems, service\nis often left to the end-user or to the myriad of small electrical/mechanical\nrepair shops that exist in the larger towns. Larger systems will be\nserviced either by the wholesaler or retailer of these systems. It should\nbe noted that a recent Presidential decree has required all firms that\n\t\nsell equipment to the government or to projects financed by the government\nto maintain a service department and a supply of spare parts that is\ncapable of maintaining all equipment that is sold. Many distributors are\ncurrently finding a shortage of skilled mechanics as a consequence of this\nregulation.\nEnd-users tend to be highly price sensitive and often a price difference\nof It in comparable products will result in the purchase of the cheaper unit.\nThe Japanese have recognized this price sensitivity and have allowed their\ndealers only a 3% mark-up on the cost of the system in order to capture a\nlarger proportion of the market. The Philippine government does try to\nencourage the local production of electrical generation equipment by allowing\nusually a 10\xe2\x80\xa2 premium on these systems before selecting the cheaper unit.\n\n7-5\n\nkj\n\nrr\n7.4 Current Generation Equipment Competition\nPresently, U.S. suppliers lead in the motor generator set field, which\nmay be an advantage to American photovoltaic manufacturers. The three major\nsuppliers are Caterpillar, GM Detroit Diesel and Cummins. Their long history\nin the market, good service organisations and extensive availability of spare\n\nparts puts them in the lead. The sales of those firms are concentrated in\nthe range of diesel generator sets below 1,000 KW, which constitute the bulk\nof the market. For diesel sets above 1,000 KW, major suppliers include European firms such as Mirrlees Blackstone, MAN, and Pielstick mainly because these\nfirms are able to arrange financing for end-uers. In the very small generator set range, gasoline engines are used. Leading suppliers for that range\ninclude Briggs z Stratton, Honda, and Wisconsin. DHR has found that in the\nagricultural sector, the near term competition is most likely to be gasoline\nengines rather than gasoline generators, particularly in irrigation/pumping\napplications.\nIt is common for alternators and motors to be imported separately and\nassembled locally for tariff purposes. Leading alternator suppliers include\nDelco of the U.S., Hawker-Siddley, Brush, and Stanford of the United Kingdom.\nLeading motor suppliers, in addition to Caterpillar, Detroit Diesel and Cummins, include Ford and Lister, both from the United Kingdom, Hercules of the\nU.S., Slanzi of Italy and MAN of West Germany. British suppliers have a\nstrong position in the market as a result of their long history of market\npromotion and frequent visits by their representatives. German motors are\nknown for their quality and reliability. Italian suppliers enter the market\nby quoting competitive prices and liberal credit terms. The Board of Invest-\n\nment (DOI) has also given permission for two firms, Izuzu Motor Co. and West\nGermany\'s MAN to manufacture all ranges of diesel engines in the Philippines.\n\n7-6\n\nV\n\n7.5 Foreign P/v c\'rmo*tition\nAustralian, French, German and Japanese firms have approached local\nentrepreneurs as to their interest in photovoltaic systems. The French\ncurrently have two small 200 watt demonstration systems located in the\nProvince of Bulacan outside of Manila:\' These demonstrations are under the\nauspices of the United Nations Development Program in cooperation with the\nPhilippine\'s Non-Conventional Energy Center and the Farm System Development\nCorporation. The German government has proposed a $4 million photovoltaic\nsystem for the Province of Illocos del *forte for electrifying a village.\nAn agreement in principal has been worked out between the German government,\nthe Non-Conventional Energy Center and the Technology Resource Center.\nThe system is expected to be in place by the and of 1981. AEG-Telefunken\nwill supply the photovoltaic system and train Filipino technicians to operate\nand maintain the system. An Australian firm is selling used American\nsolar panels to a Filipino entrepreneur.\nIn addition to foreign competitors, American photovoltaic manufacturers\nmay also in the future face local Filipino competition. A large, local, industrial firm and a large commercial bank have expressed interest-in investing in\nAmerican photovoltaic firms and establishing an indigenous photovoltaic industry based on American technology. A number of smaller firms and entrepreneurs\nalso expressed an interest in joint ventures with American firms primarily in\nthe importatic,^ of photovoltaic systems. These entrepreneurs have also a p\n-proachedforeignphotvltaicmanufctureswith eposibltyoflca mnufacture of photovoltaic systems.\n*An additional two P /V pumps were supplied by an American manufacturer.\n\n7-7\n\n1\n\nEI{\t\nOf the dealers interviewed, those presently carrying American motor\ngenerator sets appeared to be willing to retail American photovoltaic systems. \t\nHowever, to remain competitive in the field nearly all retailers expressed\nthe belief that American firms must offer liberal credit terms and low dealer\nmargins to prevent the Japanese or Europeans from taking over and controlling the\nmarket. The advantages of using these dealers as part of a Philippine\nmarketing effort is that they are 1) familiar with the electric generation\nmarket, 2) familiar in dealing with American firms, and 3) usually have\na trained core of service representatives knowledgeable about electric\nrepair.\n7.6 Climate for Investment\nThe Philippine government has accorded the local manufacture of\nphotovoltaic systems "pioneer industry" status. All projects under this status:\n\n"...shall be accorded priority by all government-owned\nor controlled financing institutions such as, but not\nlimited to, the Central Bank of the Philippines,\nDevelopment Bank of the Philippines, Philippine National\nBank and applications for financial assistance submitted\nfor these projects shall be given preferential consideration in the matter of collateral requirements, rediscounting and other requirements in order to facilitate\nthe early establishment of the projects. Furthermore,\ncosts incurred in the establishment and construction of\nnon-conventional energy conversion facilities or equipment\nduly certified by the Ministry of Energy# may, at the option\nof the taxpayer, be directly chargeable to expenses and\nshall be fully deductible as such from gross income in\nthe year wherein such expenses were incurred."\n\nIn addition to the above, there are also a number of incentives regarding\nFilipino equity, tax exemptions, remittance of profits, etc. that result in\n\nSource: Investment Opportunities in the Philippines, Board of Investments,\nSeptember 1980.\n# Certifying authority is the Center for Non-conventional Energy Development.\n+\t\n\n7.9\n\nR\n\n,\n\n^^--..-na,.-.n,....s^w\xe2\x80\xa2-...-. \t\n\n..\t\n\n-F -R-...^-.-,^v.a.-_...^\t\n\n..-- ,--^+^.r^,^,A,^,IN^,,,,R \t\n\n-^. _ ,.r :..,^ -F...\n\n^.-_--.-.....^,.^^.....^..A...wn..,,,^\n\nan exceptional business climate for investment by foreign firms in the local\nmanufacture of photovoltaics (See Appendix 8). In fact, one American\nphotovoltaic manufacturer has filed a letter of intent to establish a\nlocal subsidiary that would eventually manufacture both the silicon cells\nand photovoltaic modules. An additional incentive to locate a photovoltaic\nmanufacturing facility in the Philippines is its membership in ASEAN - a\npotentially large market that could be developed from the Philippines.\n7.7 Standards and Regulations\nGenerally, all U.S.\n\nstandards are acceptable in the Philippines. The\n\ntechnical orientation of industry and government in the country is toward\nthe U.S. as a result of the long history of U.S. presence. Local standards\nare most often based on U.S. standards.\nThe power supply is 60 hertz, 220-240 volts and 440-480 volts.\nTransmission is 69 KV (kilovolts), 115 KV, 138 KV, and 240 KV.\nStandards most commonly specified in National Power Corporation,\nNational Electrification Administration and for power and transmission projects\ninclude the following:\nt\n\nUL - Underwriters\' Laboratories Inc. (U.S.)\nNEMA - National Electrical Manufacturers Association (U.S.)\nREA - Rural Electrification Administration (U.S.)\n\n7\n\nASTM - American Society for Testing and Materials (U.S.)\nIEC - International Electrotechnical Commission ( Europe)\nPTS - Philippine Trade Standards\nPEC - Philippine Electrical Code\nIPCEA - Insulated Power Cable Engineers Association (U.S.)\n\nf\n\n^ V v\n\n7-9\n\nj\nr\nDIN - German National Standards\n\n(hest Germany)\n\nJIS - Japan Institute of Standards (Japan)\nANSI - American National Standards Institute\nEEI - Edison Electric Institute\n7.8 Tariff Rates\nEnergy equipment and supplies imported into the Philippines are subject\nto customs duties and sales taxes (see Appendix C). Customs duties are contained in the Tariff and Customs Code of the Philippines. The basis of\ndutiable value is described in the Code as follows:\n"The dutiable value of an imported article subject to\nan ad valorem rate of duty shall be based on the home\nconsumption value or price (excluding internal excise\ntaxes) of same, like or similar articles, as bought\nand sold or offered for sale freely in the usual\nwholesale quantities in the ordinary course of trade,\nin the principal markets of the country from where\nexported on the date of exportation to the Philippines,\nor where there is none on such date, then on the home\nconsumption value or price nearest to the date of\nexportation including the value of all containers,\ncoverings and/or packings of any kind and all other\ncosts, charges and expenses incident to placing the\narticle in the condition ready for shipment to the\nPhilippines plus ten (10) percent of such home\nconsumption value or price."\nIn addition to import duties, energy systems equipment imports are\nsubject to a compensating sales tax as specified in the New Sales Tax\nLaw (NIRC). The rate for energy systems equipment as defined in this study\nwould be 10% calculated on a base of the total landed cost plus a 25%\nmark-up. The calculation would be as follows:\n\na\ni\n7-10\n\n\t\nt\n\n(Home Consumption\n\nvalue)\t\n\n+ ( lot of Nam consumption + ( Postage, Commission\nvalue\t\ni Other Charges)\n\n+ (Custom Duty) w Total Landed Cost\n(Total Landed Cost) + ( 25% of Total Landed cost) x 10%\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nsales Tax\n\nIt should be noted that photovoltaic & as a pioneer industry would be\n\'f\t\n\nexempted from all taxes under the National Internal Revenue Code, except\nincome tax, on a diminishing percentage. However, it is not clear whether\nimported\n\nphotovoltaic systems would be exempt from import duties and sales\n\ntaxes. It is likely that imported photovoltaic systems would be taxed if a\nlocal photovoltaic manufacturing capacity existed and therefore imported\nsystems would be at a price disadvantage.\nBalance of system components required for photovoltaic systems would be\ntariffed. Presently, dealers of power generation equipment import\nalternators and motors separately from generators to avoid paying the higher\ntariff for the complete system. In all likelihood, American firms exporting\nphotovoltaic systems to the Philippines will have to ship the balance of\nsystem components separately from photovoltaic malules to avoid paying a\nsimilar import duty on the complete system.\n7.9 Conclusion\nPresently, American manufacturers of photovoltaic systems have several\nadvantages in developing the Philippine market. First, there is an established\ndealer/service/wholesaler network that is extensive and familiar with American\nproducts and business practices. Second, photovoltaic systems have been\n\n\xe2\x80\x94=\t\n\ngranted a number of tax incentives in order to promote the use of these\nsystems in the Philippines. Third, there are a number of entrepreneurs\n\n6ti\t\n`r.^ 1\t\nn\n\n\xc2\xb5\t\ni\n\n711\n\nwilling to invest in local production of photovoltaic systems in ;oint\nventures with American firms. Finally, as a pioneer industry photovoltaic\nsystems will be granted priority access to capital from all governmentowned or controlled financing institutions.\nPotential disadvantages to American development of the Philippine\nmarket is the presence by foreign firms currently being established through\ndemonstration projects.\n\ni\n\n4\n7-12\n\nAPPENDIX A\nPhilippine Key contacts\n\nDr. Jose A. In particular, it focuses upon the incentives that are\navailable and the regulations that one can expect to encounter in producing\nand/or selling photovoltaics in the Philippines.\nThree cases are developed in the course of this discussion, paralleling the three basic types of business involvement for a foreign (non-Filipino owned) firm in the Philippines. The first case looks at a foreign\nfirm producing photovoltaic products outside the Philippines and exporting\nthem to that country. In the second case, the firm both produces and sells\nphotovoltaic goods inside the Philippines. Finally, in the third case,\nthe firm not only produces and sells photovoltaics within the Philippines,\nbut also exports some of the Philippine-produced goods abroad. Incentives\nand regulations differ for these three scenarios, and these differences are\ndelineated in this examination.\nThis discussion is not intended to serve as a comprehensive manual on\ndoing business in the Philippines. Rather, its purpose is to outline some\nof the major incentives and regulations, acting as a springboard from which\nfurther inquiries for more detailed information can ensue. Accordingly,\nadditional references on specific topics will be furnished throughout the\ndiscussion.\nTable 1 provides background information on the Philippines and its\neconomy. More detailed information can be found in the SGV Group\'s Philippines: An Economic Profile 1979. Having briefly examined the Philippines\nin general, we now turn to the first case -- a foreign firm exporting\nphotovoltaic products to the Philippines.\n\nB-1\n\n1\n;\n\nTABLE 1\nGENERAL PHILIPPINES CHARACTERISTICS\n\nPOPULATION: 42.1 million according to the 1975 Census (Average\nannual growth rate of 2.7 \xe2\x99\xa6 since 1970)\nLANGUAGE: Eighty -seven dialects spoken; chiefly\nIlocano, Taaaloa and Cebuano. Enalish\nis also spoken.\nCURRENCY: Peso (P), U.S. $1 - P7.25 (January 1, 1981)\n\nGNP: P86.7 billion, or approximately U.S. $12 billion,\nin 1979 (in 1972 prices) (An average 6.7% real\nannual growth rate between 1972-1979)\nPRINCIPAL INDUSTRIES: Agriculture, Forestry, Fisheries (accounted for\napproximately 608 of labor force in 1977)\nLABOR FORCE: Approximately 16.9 million in 1977 (5s growth rate\nsince 1977)\nTAXES: Corporate Income Tax\nIndividual Income Tax\nExcise Taxes\nLocal Taxes\n(plus others)\nFOREIGN INVESTMENT: Total net inflow of foreign equity investment,\naccording to National Census and Statistics Office,\nof P1,599.2 million (U.S. $216 million) in 1977\n(U.S. a major source of foreign investment).\n\nSources: SGV & Co., Philippines: An Economic Profile 1979\nSGV 6 Co., Doing Business in the Philippines 1980\nPrice Waterhouse, Doing Business in the Philippines, March, 1978.\n\nB-2\n\nt\n9.2 Case 1 -- Export to the Philippines\nIn the first case, a foreign firm is exporting photovoltaic product\nto the Philippines. Here, while the firm may choose to set up an office\n\niL the Philippines to coordinate its activity, there is no actual produc\nof goods within the Philippines.\nA firm exporting to the Philippines is subject to a variety of reg%\nlations. For example, it is prohibited from engaging in retail trade. 7\nRetail Trade Nationalization Act restricts retail trade, either direct c\nindirect, to those firms wholly owned by Philippine citizens. I The fire\npermitted to use foreign trademarks in the Philippines, however, such tj\nmarks or tradenames must be registered with the Philippine Patent Office\n(PPO). This registration is valid for 20 years from the date of issuanc\nso long as the registrant files an affidavit of use with the PPO within one\n(1) year following Cie fifth, tenth and fifteenth anniversaries of the registration. However, in order for registration to be granted, the firm\'s\nhome country must grant similar privileges to Filipinos, i.e., reciprocity.2\nMethods of payment for imports are also regulated. Philippine imports\nmay be financed by either letters of credit (L/Cs), documents against\nacceptance (D/A), or open account (C/A) arrangements. The Philippine\n\xe2\x80\xa2\nCentral Bank must approve importations maturing beyond 360 days, as such\nimportations are treated as foreign borrowings. 3 Further detail on payment\nprocedures can be found in Price Waterhouse, Doing Business in the Philipp\'nes (March, 1978). \'\ni\nIn order to comply with Customs and International regulations, a number\nof documents are required for import of goods. These include: \t\n\n1 SGV i Co., Doing Business in the Philippines 1980, p. 29.\n2 Guerrero\n\n(\n\n\t\n\ni\n\n6 Torres, Primer on Doing Business in the Philippines 1980. p. 27.\n\n3Price Waterhouse i Co., Doing Business in the Philippines, March, 1978,\np. 43.\nB-3\na\n\nn\n\n6\nE\n}\nt\nt\n\ne\n\nConsular Invoice for shipments over 9500 in export valuer\n\ne Certificate of Origin;\ne Commercial Invoices\ne Bill of Lading;\ne Inward Cargo Manifest;\ne Delivery Permits\ne Central Bank Release Certificate\xe2\x80\xa2\nIn setting up operations in the Philippines, if desired, the firm may\nestablish and register any of the following:\n1)\n\nJoint Venture Corporation\n\n2)\n\nBranch\n\n3)\n\nSubsidiary\n\n4)\n\nRepresentative Office\n\nThe firm may enter into a joint venture corporation with Philippine residents, contributing equity, subject to Philippine participation regulations.\nAlternatively, a branch is considered an extension of a foreign enterprise,\nand is fully controlled by the parent company. All the assets of the firm\nmay be held liable for the operations of the branch. A subsidiary, while\nnormally mostly owned by the parent company, has a juridicial personality\nseparate and distinct from its parent company, with the parent company .\ninsulated from subsidiary liabilities. Finally, the firm can establish a\nrepresentative office, having a maximum of ten personnel of which only two\ncan be expatriates. 5 Tax information of the various forms of operation can\nbe found in SGV i Co., Doing Business in the Philippines 1980.\n\n4SGV i Co., Doing Business in the Philippines 1980, p. 36.\n5Guerrero & Torres, op. cit., pp. 3-8.\n1\nB-4\n\nA\n\nTable 2 provides a summary of the information in the first case.\n\'\t\n\nTABLE 2\t\nCase 1 -- Export to the Philippines\nGeneral Information\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Prohibited from engaging in retail trade.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Permitted to use trademarks and tradenames subject\nto reciprocity.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Payment for exports via letters of credit, documents against acceptance, or open account arrangements.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Importations maturing beyond 360 days must be approved by the Central Bank.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 A variety of documents required for goods to be\nimported (Consular Invoice on shipments over\n#500, Certificate of Origin, Commercial invoice,\nBill of Lading, Inward Cargo Manifest, Delivery\nPermit, Central Bank Release Certificate).\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Four types of Philippines business vehicles can be\nestablished -- joint venture corporation, branch,\nsubsidiary, representative office.\nB.3 Case 2 -- Produce and Sell in the Philippines\nIn the second case, a foreign firm is producing and selling photovoltaic products in the Philippines. All goods that are manufactured are sold\nin the Philippines; none of the firm\'s Philippine production is exported.\nPhotovoltaics is considered a "pioneer" area in the Philippines, and\nas a consequence, firms engaged in producing photovoltaic products qualify\nfor special benefits and incentives once registered with the Philippine\nBoard of Investments (BOI). A foreign firm is permitted to possess 100%\nof capital stock in a Philippine photovoltaics firm, whereas only 30% is\ngenerally allowed for firms engaged in non-priority activities. However,\nit should be noted that "pioneer" enterprises are required to attain Filipino status (60% Filipino ownership) within 30 years unless granted a 10year extension by the DOI.6\n\n6Philippine Board of Investments, Questions and Answers on Foreign Investments in the Philippines, June, 1980, pp. 4-5.\na-s\n\nP\n\n\t\nr\n1\n\nPhotovoltaic enterprises also qualify for a variety of benefits under\nthe Investment Incentives Act (R.A. 5186) as amended by Presidential Decree\nNo. 1584. Table 3 summarises the incentives provided to firms; specific\nprovisions of the Act can be found in the B0I\'s, The Investment and Export\nIncentives Act. Further, a firm is able to qualify for additional incentives if it locates its production, processing and manufacturing plants in an\narea designated as less developed. The additional incentives include:\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Tax allowance to the extent of actual investment but not to\nexceed 301 of taxable income;\nExemption from General Banking Act collateral requirement on\nloans against real estate security;\ne Exemption from the payment of filing, processing and all other\nfees of BOI and Security and Exchange Commission if total assets\nare less than 1 million pesos.?\nAs p%rt of its effort to respond to its need for greater self-sufficiency in energy resources, the Philippines extends additional benefits to\nthose cited above to photovoltaic enterprises as part of its 1980 Energy\nPriorities Program. Projects registered under the program are accorded\npriority by all government-owned or controlled financing institutions,\nsuch as the Central Bank and the Development Bank, and are given preferential consideration on collateral, rediscounting and other requirements in\norder to facilitate the early establishment of the projects.8\nFinally, photovoltaic enterprises, as are all foreign investors and\nenterprises, are given several basic rights and guarantees to ensure the\nsafety of foreign investments. photovoltaic products\nin the Philippines. It may either sell part of its goods in the Philippines and part abroad, or export all of its Philippine pr(,\xe2\x80\xa2du:tion.\nIn an effort to develop the country\'s export trade, the Philippines\noffer benefits and incentives in addition to those delineated in Scenario\n2, to photovoltaic enterprises that produce for export. Photovoltaic\nenterprises that export at least 70 \xe2\x99\xa6 of their total production are allowed\nan additional 10 years to attain Filipino status, 40 years as opposed to\n30 years. Further, these firms are still eligible for a 10-year extension\nby the Sol. Moreover, if a firm exports 1001 of its production, it need\nnot attain Filipino status.9\nPhotovoltaic firms registered with the BOI as a "registered export\nproducer," also qualify for additional benefits under the Investment Incentives Act and the Export Incentives Act (R.A. 6135) as amended by Presidential Decree No. 1646. Table 5 summarizes benefits for the three cases we have examined. incentives are\nprovided in many forms, primarily by lowering tax expenses and generating\nan environment conducive to firm operation.\nPhotovoltaics, by virtue of its "pioneer" status, qualifies for most of\nthe incentives offered, as long as goods are produced in the Philippines.\nIn addition, supplemental benefits can be received if the firm exports a\nportion of its Philippine photovoltaics production and if the firm locates\n!\t\n\nits plants in an area designated as less developed. They accept savings and checking or current accounts and other\nusual bank services like loans and trust holdings, extend domestic and\nexport letters of credit and trust receipt services. They are also\nactive in the money market and in small loans to small and medium-scale\nindustries. In the Philippines, some banks are even involved in credit\ncard operations and financing through a system of sister companies.\nCredits granted by commercial banks consist loans, advances,\ndiscounts, overdrafts, domestic bills, customer\'s liability acceptances\nand export bills.\nOf the P52,510 . 7 million total credits granted by commercial banks\nin 1977, 72.29 percent or roughly 138.0 billion went to private businesses\nand individuals. Credits granted to local governments as well as semigovernment entities reached P6,04P.8 million or 11.52 percent while\nanother 6 . 23 percent or P3,270.0 million was availed of by the national\ngovernment.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Savings and Mortgage Banks\nA savings and mortgage bank is any corporation organized primarily\nfor accumulating the small deposits of the public and reinvesting them in\nbonds, in loans secured by pledge or chattel mortgage certificates, buying\nand selling them or receiving them in payment of loans. However, a savings\nbank cannot engage in foreign exchange trading, nor can it open letters of\ncredit. It can grant all kinds of loans extendable by commercial banks,\nexcept character loans or overdraft lines which are payable on a medium or\nlong - term basis.\nTotal resources of savings banks amounted to P2 , 810.4 million as of\nthe end of 1977. Total loans outstanding of savings and mortgage banks on\nthe other hand, amounted to P1 , 954.9 million broken down as follows:\nagricultural - P200 million; commercial - P2 million; industrial - P22\nmillion; real estate - P1,112 million; consumption - P195 million; and\nsundry - P424 million. Investments in bonds and other securities meanwhile reached P415 . 3 million. Total loans and investments made by the\nsavings banks reached P2,370.2 million in 1977.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Rural Banks\n\n\t\n\nRural banks are generally small regional unit banks. In terms of\nassets and size, they are smaller than either the savings or comiercial\nbanks. They perform the same savings and loan services as thrift banks\nbut rural banks specialize in extending small loans to farmers. Some, are\n\ns\nr\n\nk\nG\n\nr-1\n\nalso authorized to accept demand deposits or checking accounts.\nAll rural banks are privately owned, but they receive financial and\ntechnical assistance and incentives from the government. Their\nmain sources of funds therefore are the deposits of rural savers\nand the financial assistance of the government.\nAbout !977.5 million or 47.3 percent of the total loans granted\nwas made under the supervised credit scheme. The balance of\nP1,088.2 million or 52.7 percent was extended under the non-supervised credit program.\nAgricultural loans accounted for 87.1 percent of the total\nloans granted for the year. Commercial loans represented 8.7 percent, while industrial and other loans constituted 2.7 and 1.5\npercent, respectively. Investment in securities on the other hand,\nreached !832.1 million.\nThe rural banking system aims to accomplish the followin g goals\nand objectives: 1) to continue to support the food production program and other developmental projects of the government through\nactive participation in the various special financing programs;\n2) to exert more efforts in mobilizing savings and promoting capital\nformation to be able to provide more and better service to small\nfarmers and producers in the rural areas; 3) to step up participation\nin the fourth Central Bank-International Bank for Reconstruction and\nDevelopment (CB-IBRD) rural credit project to meet the increasing\ndemands of agriculture and industries for medium and long-term\ncredit; 4) to cooperate with the government in devising ways and\nmeans to reduce the percentage of arrearages of the system to a more\nmanageable level; and 5) to pursue a program of developing managerial\nskills and personnel capabilities through sustained training programs\nand seminars, so that rural banks will be in a better position to\ncope with their day-to-day operational problems.\ne Land Bank of the Philippines\nThe Land Bank is charged with Vie responsibility of giving\nfinancial support to three of the more important aspects of the\nagrarian reform program namely: 1) the transfer of ownership of\nagricultural lands covered by the agrarian reform from landlord to\ntenant-farmers; 2) the effort to help farmer-beneficiaries of land\nreform increase their productivity and income; and 3) the redirection\nof land-owner resources to industry or other productive endeavors.\nMoreover, the bank in its capacity as administrator of two agricultural guarantee funds (the Land Bank Agricultural Guarantee Fund\n(LB-AGF) and the Agricultural Guarantee Fund (AGF-R.A. 6390)), extends\ncoverage on certain types of loans granted to farmers by selected\nlending institutions. Through its guarantee operations, the bank\nmakes it possible for farmers with meager resources to undertake productive activities by encouraging lending institutions to extend\ncredit without being too strict about collateral requirements.\n\nD-2\n\na Development Banks\nExcept for the Development Bank of the Philippines (DBP), development banks are usually small in size and operate as regional banks.\nWhile they rely heavily on local or foreign borrowings through a program\nof bond issues, they are also authorized to generate deposit funds from\nthe public as their source of funds for the loans and financing services\nthey provide.\nDevelopment banks usually provide short-to-long term financing for\nproductive enterprises on a secured basis. Compared with commercial\nbanks which can loan or finance an enterprise without collateral or\nsecurity for the amount borrowed, development banks and thrift banks are\nsecurity or collateral oriented.\nForemost among the development banks is the Development Bank of the\nPhilippines. It is entrusted with the responsibilities of providing\ncredit facilities for the rehabilitation, development and expansion of\nagriculture and industry, the reconstruction of the national economy,\nand the promotion and establishment of private development banks in provinces and cities.\nAs of year-end 1977, total resources of the DBP rose to new levels P15.7 billion as against 112.7 billion in 1976.\nInvestments in securities also increased by P573 million from 92.1\nbillion to 92.6 billion. Of these outstanding investments, 92.12 billion\nor 81 percent are investments in government securities and P503 million or\n19 percent are private securities. Loan portfolio as of December 31, 1977,\nlikewise increased by 91.8 billion or 23 percent from !7,870.2 million to\nP9,665.9 million.\nThe thrust of lending operations of the bank during the period under\nreview continued to be on countryside development as well as on small and\nmedium-scale industry financing. Under the DBP\'s countryside development\nprogram, equal attention is given to agricultural and industrial projects.\nIn agriculture, priority is given to projects geared towards self-sufficiency\nin food, production of raw materials for local industry needs and diversification of agricultural exports.\n0 Non-Bank Financial Institutions\nThe non-bank sector, while extending financial services to the commercial and industrial sectors of the economy, has a different source of funds.\nNon-banks cannot generate deposit funds from the public, thus they generally\nresort to stockholder\'s funds and deposit substitute liabilities to fund\nlending activities. Deposit substitutes are financial instruments held by\nthe non-banking sector as part of its funds. These include commercial\npapers such as stocks and bonds, short and long-term debt instruments, and\nother credit instruments obtained from money market activities. The nonbank sector also borrows funds to finance its services.\n\na\nf\nD-3\n\n(\nr\n\nNon-bank financial institutions include: the National Investment\nand Development Corporation (NIDC), Private Development Corporation of\nthe Philippines (PDCP), BANCOM Development Corporation, Non-Stock\nSavings and Loan Associations, the Government Service Insurance System\n(GSIS), Social Security System (SSS), Agricultural Credit Administration\n(ACA), and Mutual Building and Loan Association (MBLA).\nThe NIDC, PDCP, and BANCOM Development Corporation provide primarily\nlong-term financing for expansion and modernization of productive\nventures and for facilitating short-term placements to commercial\nbanks. Stock Savings and Loan Association contributes to home savings\nand to the growing need for more savings institutions.\n- Government Non-Bank Financial Institutions\nLoanable funds of government non-bank financial institutions\n(comprising the GSIS, SSS, ACA and NIDC) released their funds in\n1977 for the following purposes: agricultural - P40 million; industrial - P40 million; consumption - P893 million; real estate P579 million; public utility - P21 million and other purposes (consisting mostly of notes receivable from financial firms) - P2,359.4\nmillion. Combined, these loans aggregated P3,932.8 million reflecting\nan expansion of 49 percent over the level of the preceding year.\nAccounting for the increase was the !1,619.2 million growth in\nother purpose loans which more than offset the decreases in loans\nfor industrial, consumption, real estate and public utility purposes.\n- Agricultural Credit Administration\nCreated under R.A. 3844, also known as the Agricultural Land\nReform Code, the Agricultural Credit Administration (ACA) was\nspecifically designed to help farmers elevate their living standards\nto make them active factors in the country\'s economic development.\nIts activities are therefore, geared at raising the economic wellbeing of the rural populace by increasing their agricultural production. To achieve this end, it provides the farmers with the necessary\ntechnical guidance and credit assistance for the cost of production\ninputs.\nThe ACA grants six types of loans, namely: production, facility,\ncommodity, marketing, operating capital and poultry loans. A major\nportion of the loans granted from 1960 to 1977 were of the production,\ncommodity and marketing types. These three types of loans, constituted\nan average of 66.7, 6.0 and 21.7 percent respectively, of the total\nloans granted in 1977 of #49.7 million.\n- Private Non-Bank Financial Institutions\n\nc\n\n1\n\nPrivate non-bank financial enterprises showed reduced lending\noperations in 1977 as loans granted totaling !1,410 million declined\nby 28 percent from the previous year\'s figure of #1,969 million.\nThese loans were released by BANCOM, PDCP, MBLA and Non-Stock SLA\nfor various purposes, namely: agricultural - P28 million; commercial - P46.2 million; real estate - P172.3 million; public utility P9.7 million; and others - P707.2 million. Except agricultural\nD-4\n\npurpose loans which expanded by more than two times, other types of\nloans registered decrements ranging from 3 percent to 83 percent\nas against their respective levels in 1976.\n- Investment Houses\nInvestment houses grant medium to long-term loans for capital\nexpansion purposes. The basic function of investment houses,\nhowever, is the underwriting and distribution of securities of\nother corporations. Commissions and serv<.ces from this function\nand earnings from money market activities are their main sources\nof funds.\nFinance companies on the other hand, are authorized to finance\nconsumer durables under installment payment plans. They usually\nextend short-to-medium term credit to manufacturers and merchants\nfor inventory, receivable financing and transport equipment leasing.\nFinancing companies usually rely on stockholders funds and cash\ngenerated from their money market activities to finance their\noperation.\nThe Private Development Corporation of the Philippines is a\ndevelopment finance institution which is committed to encourage\nthe emergence and growth of Philippine productive enterprises by\nproviding technical and financial assistance through the purchase\nof equity investments and expertise.\nThe BANCOM Development Corporation extends assistance to\nprojects which are viewed to be primary contributors to the acceleration of the country\'s economic development. Most of these\nprojects are registered with the Board of Investments and are\ninvolved in the production of essential products like petroleum,\nmining, wood, pharmaceutical, paper, sugar and coconut. Like\nthe PDCP, it also engages in money market operations and it has\nactively engaged in the organization of the Money Market Association of the Philippines.\n\nD-S\n\nAPPENDIX E\nASSUMPTIONS USED IN THE FINANCIAL AND ECONOMIC ANALYSES\nThis appendix lists the methodology, data and assumptions used in\nthe financial and economic analyses described in Chapter S. Agriculture accounts for 30\xe2\x80\xa2 of\nthe GDP, more than 50% of employment, and close to 60% of export earnings.\nThe Philippines is\nan archipelago, divided\n\no\'\n\ninto three island groups:\n\no\n\nRAINFALL\nBY ZONES\n\nLuzon, Visayas and\n%\n\nMindanao.
