Mir.dano\nis the second largest and\n\nQ season lastnp\t arty\t from one\n\n0\n\nVisayan\ne\t\n\n\'\n\nlies in the south. The\nother nine islands, the\nVisayan, constitute 29%\nof the total country area.\nMindanao\n\nTo a large extent the\n\n0\n\nPhilippines is a mountain-\n\n, te a ^I\n\nous country with ranges\nlying close to and para-\n\no\n\nno\n\nO\n\n400\t\n\nllel with the coastlines.\nOne of the main impediments\n\nF\xe2\x80\xa2-1\n\n\t\n\nata WIM\nao \xe2\x80\xa2no.+tfats\n\nj\n\nto increased production of food is bad weather. The country is normally\nvisited by about 20 tropical cyclones (storms and typhoons) annually\ncausing damage to crops and livestock. Your types of climate are characterised by either the length of absence of a dry season, or by the timing\nof maximum rainfall.. The western part has a dry season lasting from December to May. The eastern part has no dry season but a very pronounced\nrainfall from December to February. The central part of the country has\ntwo climates: in the north a short dry season which lasts from 1-3 months;\nin the south rainfall is fairly well distributed throughout the year but\nthe total amount is less than that in the eastern part of the country. The\nmean annual rainfall for the entire country is 2,530mm.\nAgriculture, fishery and forestry contributed, in 1979, 26% of the\ngross national product. Almost half of the Philippine\'s population is\nagricultural (21,529,000). Sixty-five to seventy percent of this population\nare rice and/or maize semi-sufficient farmers. Eight million hectares are\nclassified as arable and permanent cropland, providing less than .4 ha. per\nagricultural caput. Average farm size has been estimated at close to 3h\nhectares. Land is perhaps the biggest constraint in Philippine agriculture;\nnext comes water; then comes labor.\nCentral Luzon is the nation\'s rice bowl, with the bulk of irrigated\nland occurring in this section. The northern regions (Ilocos, Cagayan\nValley and Central Luzon) and the western Visayas are the most important\nareas of intensive crop farming. Large tracts of arable land are left\nfallow in the eastern Visayas and Mindanao. Large-scale operators everywhere are involved in sugar, bananas and pineapple for export, and are\nincreasingly taking over poultry and swine production. Smallholders\nprimarily produce food grains for domestic use and coconut for export.\nLivestock: Most of the cattle are raised by small-scale farmers,\n\neach with one to several head. Meat consumption is low and native cattle\nare raised primarily as work animals, second in importance only to the\ncarabao. This water buffalo plows the rice paddies and other lard, is a\nmain source of transportation, and an emergency source of cash. Carsbaos\nalso account for 2/3 the beef supply. Most animals are maintained by\ngrazing on fallow fields and harvest stubble with a supplementary feeding\n\nF-2\n\n\t\n\n+etr .,..,...,.^._...__ \t\n\n____._\xe2\x80\xa2_^.\n\nof rice straw. \t\n\nI\n\nThe Philippine dairy farming industry is wry limited. Presently\n\n{\t\n\nthere are only a few commercial dairy operations.\nPoultry production ( eggs and meat) has become highly c =arcialized\n(65% of the chicken population) in locations near metropolitan areas,\nand continues to expand. The Philippines is now self-sufficient in\npoultry, and increased development is being hindered by the high cost of\nprotein feed ingredients which are mostly imported.\nCommerical piggeries currently account for 10% of the country\'s pork\nproduction, the balance being in small backyard operations. A few Corporations operate large holdings\n\n( 10,000-15,OOOha.)\n\nand corporations have\n\nset up a few feed-lot fattening businesses to utilize waste from primary\nenterprises. Total number of cattle at a given time is about 6,000 head.\nForestry:\n\nForest products rank among the country \' s top four categor-\n\nies in export value. Mahoganies make up the bulk of lumber exports.\nFishing: Although the Philippines lies in a fertile fishing belt,\n\nthe domestic fishing has remained underdeveloped. Obsolete techniques,\ninadequate refrigeration and marketing facilities, and lack of investment\ncapital have handicapped the industry so that production does not meet\neven domestic needs. The annual fish catch in 1977 totaled 1,510,789 MT\nwhich was wostly anchovies, mackerels, sea bass, sardines, tuna, bonito\nand shark.\nSmall-scale, traditional fishermen in the Philippines use gear not\nrequiring boats, or boats of three tons or less. More than one-third of\nthe fishermen are dependent upon mototized bancas (consuming an average of\n1200\n\nliters of gasoline each every year), but almost all fishing gear is\n\ntotally non-mechanical. (The primary exception to this are the artificial\nlights used in basnig-fishery where fish are guided into surrounding nets\n\t\n\nby the light. Ninety percent of the sardines are caught in this method.)\n\nr\n\nTraditional fishermen fish both inland and ocean waters. There are\napproximately 600,000 traditional fishermen located in some 10 , 000 coastal\nfishing villages throughout the country. They use innumerable techniques\nsuch as brush traps, tubular split bamboo traps, bamboo wiers of elaborate\n\n\t\n\ni\nt\n\nF-3\n\ndesign and size, "Kalaskas" boats which have bamboo and rattan root scaressweepers, dkimming nets, "Sapyan" shoal nets, purse seines, and tiny cover\npots for use in the paddy fields.\nFishing villages are generally without wharves, cold storage facilities, ice plants, or fish markets. In fact, fishermen are dependent neon\nthose who market their catch. The relationship between fisherman and\nbuyer is called "saki." While suki has nutually beneficial aspects,\nfishermen often end up indepted to buyers and boat owners.\nBy presidential decree in 1972, the country undertook the land reform\ntask of transferring ownership of farmland to about half the country\'s\nmillion landless tenants. By September 1979, the Ministry of Agrarian\nReform reported having issued 427,149 Certificates of Land Transfer (CLT)\nto 308,068 tenants cultivating 525,590 hectares.\n(2) Agricultural Development Plans\nThe long tezm development goal is that of achieving self-sufficiency\nin agricultural production. High priority, therefore, is centered upon\nfood production and distribution, fisheries, livestock, and related\nagricultural activities. The main thrust is the production of cheap but\n\nhighly nutritious\n\nfoods that can also generate export earnings. The\n\ngovernment has launched the Bakahang Barangay (backyard cattle raising)\nproject and has implemented a Dairy Industry Development Act to attain\nself-sufficiency in beef by 1985 and cut dairy imports. The Ministry is\nalso making efforts to establish crops traditionally imported, such as\nsoybeans, wheat, and cotton.\nThe Philippines has reached self-sufficiency in rice, and government\nefforts are now on increased production of yellow maize, and sorghum.\nThe Philippine Coconut Authority (PCA) has started a program for replanting\nold areas and planting improved hybrid vazieties. A plant for production\nof soybean products is being constructed which will require 500 tons of\nsoybeans during the first three years of operation, 50% of which is to be\nlocally grown soybeans. Banana hectarage are planned for a 25% increase,\nland allocated to producers according to past performance.\n\nw\n\nF-4\n\nI\ni\n4\n\n(3)\n\nincome Distribution\n\na Seventy-four percent of farm families in 1971 did not receive enough\ninoome to meet the food poverty\n\nthreshold, as\n\ncalculated by the Bureau of\n\nCensus and Statistics using A.S. Abrera\'s "Philippine Poverty Thresholds"\ndocument. Poverty distribution was relatively even across all eleven\nislands, only falling down to 60% for farm families in Central Luzon. The\nbulk of those farmers with the lowest incomes were the 40% of the nation\'s\ncultivators who have less than two hectares of land.\n\n(4) Credit, Cooperatives, and Extension Services\nCredit: Most farm investment is financed by non-institutional means\nby the farmer---through relatives, landlords, or private moneylenders.\nProduction credit is granted by rural banks and the Philippine National\nBank (the latter is the main source of credit for sugar planters). An\nagency for small farmer credit, the Agricultural Credit Administration,\naccounted for only 1% of total production credit in 1971. Marketing credit\nis provided by the government-owned Philippine National Bank and by private\ncommercial banks.\nInstitutional credit has been primarily reserved for larger commercial\noperations.\nParticipation in cooperatives has been relatively minor, with most\nactivity taking place in communal irrigation works, purchasing cooperatives,\nor loosely structured organizations owning warehouses and milling equipment.\nThe problem of agricultural marketing in the Philippines is one of distributing products in such a way the farmer gets rewarded for his labor. This\napplies equally to export products. For this purpost. the organization of\nfarmers into marketing cooperatives is being encouraged and even given\ndirect assistance from the government. Farmers are asked to join precooperative groups for training in cooperative principals and practices,\nthen these groups are expected to amalgamate into area marketing co-ops.\nThe main unit of agricultural extension is the Agricultural Productivity Commission (APC) which was integrated as the Bureau of Agriculture\nExtension (BAEX) into the Department of Agriculture and Natural Aetzurces\na\nF-5\t\n\n\'\n\nI\n\nin the early 1 70\'s. Also, the Department of Agrarian Affairs (DAR) still\nmaintains an extension service in the land reform area which now encompasses such of the country. As of 1973, there was only one extension agent\nfor 1,400 farmers.\n\n(5) Storage Methods\nThe Palay (unmilled rice) crop is almost entirely solar dried. This\ntakes place both on the farm and after threshing at the mill. Some mechanical driers exist but poor siting and operating costs leave them underutilized. Losses are high from solar drying because the unequal temperature and humidity causes grain cracking, over-drying, and leaves palay\nsubject to rodents, birds and insects.\nPalay and rice are stored in sacks, with only a few bulk storage\nbins and silos available. Sack storage actually minimizes losses from\nsweating and mold damage, for bulk storage requires a strict control of\nmoisture.\nMaize is stored on the farm as whole ears, shelling it only as it is\nrequired. Little, if any, drying of shelled maize is practiced on farms.\nFew mechanical driers exist, and most mills have concrete floors upon which\ngrain is spread for solar drying.\nMost farmers do not have storage facilities for coconut. Nuts are\nsold to desiccated coconut factories. They have to be hauled to a usually\ndistant road where they are collected by the buyer. Cost of transport is\nhigh and poor road facilities lead to multiple handling of the copra by\na large number of middlemen.\nBecause of the size and dispersion of individual wholesale markets,\nviajeros (truck-buyers) buy most vegetable produce either directly from\n\nthe farmer or from a local assembler. Loosely woven bamboo baskets (an\nimportant cottage industry) are customary containers in all sizes for\nall produce.\nAn increasing share of the marketing of foocgrains, feedgrains, and\npulses has been assumed by the government under the National Grains\nAuthority. Storage facilities are owned primarily by millers as independ-\n\nF-6\n\nent warehousing activities have beco.s financially less attractive due to\nincreasing government control of marketing margins.\n\n(6) Agricultural Production\nModern crop production is generally restricted to sugarcane, pineapple and some rice. These two crops use most of the country\'s fertiliser\nand cultivation/harvesting large and small machinery.\nMost farming is done with human and animal labor. Some farmers may\nintensively plant, using hybrid seeds, fertilizers and pesticides, but\nthese practices are most likely conducted by hand. The greatest percentage\nof farming is semi-self-sufficient farming of mixed crops for domestic\nconsumption, and coconuts (often in affixed cultivation) as a case. crop. If\ncrop rotation is practiced, it is that of fallow fields, or a dry crop is\nplanted on the same piece of land following a wet season crop. A lowland\nrice crop, for instance, may be harvested from November to February, depending upon the variety, and after rice harvest the field may be planted to\ncorn, tobacco, vegetables... or all three.\n\nSugar cane\n\n7\xe2\x80\xa2 of the arable lands a production of 2.4 million MT\nin the 1 78- 1 79 season; 2nd in export value with $168.7\nmullion\n\nSugar cane is the largest user of pesticides, herbicides, and farm\nmachinery in the Philippines. (Quantitative date, however, is lacking.)\n\nI\n\nMechanization, however, is only on the large farms. Over 90\xe2\x80\xa2 of farms\ngrowing sugar cane are under 50 hectares and have little or no mechanical\nequipment. The sugar industry uses all kinds of fuel including firewood.\nIn fact, the largest commercial consumption of firewood is the sugar\nindustry.\nMost sugar farms are in Central Luzon and the western Visayas. Hand\nlabor is still used even on the mechanized farms. Indeed, tise Pakiao\nlabor system is used on nearly 80% of the sugar cane haciendas. This\nsystem consists of setting a flat rate per hectare for a given operation\n(e.g., planting, weeding, or ratooning).\nt\nk\t\nF-7\t\n\nq\n\ni\n\nMilling is dons commercially with so strict scheduling of delivery so\nthat there is a 100 loss of sugar produce between harvesting and milling\nbecause can quality declines rapidly after 49 hours of harvest. Scheduling would necessitate timely land preparation, but farmers with loss than\n\'\n\n50 hectares can ill afford the purchase of a tractor.\t\n\nMT\nCentral Luzon has the greatest wmnt, and best, land for paddy rice\n(good paddy land is now priced at 2,000-5,000 per hectare). Under irrigation with M seed, and with mechanical equipment or extremely high labor\ninput, double and even triple crops can be grown. Production of this type\nis capital intensive until harvest (land preparation, irrigation, seed,\nlabor and fertilizer), then labor intensive. !land tractors save time in\ntillage and speed up cultivation for high yielding varieties, making an\nextra crop possible, but costs are high and despite low wage labor costs\nthe financial results are frequently r or unless subsidies are provided.\nThe use of small power-pumps for utilizing both surface and underground\nwater is increasing.\nThe palsy is almost entirely solar dried before milling. Ten percent\nof the crop is not milled allowing for seed and wastage (including that\nfed to livestock). Milling is generally done off the farm by\n\nMills using\n\neither the Kiskisan or Cono milling machines.\n\nCoconut\n\n42arable land devoted to this crops\n11,661,000 MT production coconut,\n2,600,000 MT production of copra.\n\nAlthough coconut is the number one export crop of the Philippines,\nwith coconut products for export totaling 2740 million in 1979, its\ncultivation continues to be that of a traditional crop. Trees are handplanted, grazing stock generally keeps weeds under control, draft animals\nare occasionally used though mostly for transport, and nuts are processed\ninto copra on the farm.\n\nF-8\n\nLarge coconut fazmers utilise tomato. Tenants provide hand labor\nand guarding, being paid in cash if they do not participate in copra\nproduction or by share of produce and annual crops cultivated under trees\nif they do. Smallholders, however, make up the bulk (70% under 4 ha.) of\ncoconut growers.\nFor making copra, the nuts are husked, cut into halves and dried.\nAfter the first drying, the nut neat is scooped out of the shell and dried\nfor a second time. This is done in the sun (104) or in "taF,ahans".\n(Tapahans are grills over an open fire with coconut shells and husks used\nas fuel.)\nGood quality copra should be :.tied for at least three days to a\nmoisture content of not more than 6%. Often this drying process time is\nspeeded up, drying the copra unequally, scorching it, and the resulting\ncopra is dark in color and susceptible to insect attack and mould. Copra\nkilns are used very infrequently in the Philippines. Inexpensive copvdrying installations used in other countries have not yet been adapted.\n\nmaize\n404 of arable and permanent land devoted\nto this crops 3,167,000 MT production\n\nMaize is grown mostly in the traditional method, even when planted\nas a sole crop. About 654 of the maize is for human consumption usually\nin the form of grits; 154 is fed to animals and poultry on the farms;\nthe remainder is manufactured into animal feed or starch and glucose off\nthe farm. production is centered in Mindanao and Visayss, with the white\nmaize variety comprising 904 of the crop grown. It is grown at all times\nof the year with double cropping being the most ccm nion.\nMilling methods are similar to those for palsy,\n\nmost\n\nmilling done\n\ncommercially. Absence of adequate feeder roads and high transport costs\nfrequently equal 504 of the farmer\'s realised value.\nVery few mechanical shellers exist, and these are owned by millers\nor "first buyers" (miller\'s agents).\n\nF-9\n\nu\n\nPine\n_ apple\n550,000 MT production, 156,600,000 export value\n(canned)\nBanana\n2,500,000 MT production; $70,700,000 export value\n\',bbacco\n82 3 4 MT production; $26,300,000 export value\n,\t\n, 4\n\nThough pineapple, banana, coffee, and tobacco are considerable export\ncrops for the Philippines, they are generally produced by hand and \'Animal\nlabor except for land preparation (and sometimes spraying and fertilizing)\nby those farmers owning tractors.\nPineapple production is expected to increase as a third commercial\ncanning plant begins operation. Bananas are now the third major agricultural export, behind coconut products and sugar.\nTraditional crop production may be better characterized by breaking\nfarmers into five major groups:\n-the indigenous kainginero (shifting cultivator\nupland farmer\n\n-the marginal kainginero\t\n-upland rice/maize farmer\n-paddy rice farmer\n-rainforest shifting cultivator\t\n\nlowland farmer\n\nThe indigenous kainginero is an upland farmer usually exercisinq\nsquatter\'s rights and oftentimes migrating as soil depletion or ego,-lor.\nlovers yields. The farmer cleans, by slash and burn, a hectare at a tame\nand subsists on a variety of annual crops. Most of his income is derived\nfrom the sale cf root crops like cassava, and what upland race isn\'t consumed domestically. He may, as well, raise a few pigs or chickens.\nThe marginal kainginero farms from one to two hectares a:. a time.\nThis farmer clears land by slash and burn to plant annuals and perennials,\noften including coffee a;id fruit trees.\nThe upland maize/rice farmer relies upon plowed land to plant maize\nin the dry season and rice in the wet season. He also cultivates in mixed\n\nF-10\n\n\t\n\ncropping coconuts, bananas, fruit trees and vegetables. Small livestock\n\ni\t\n\noften are included in the farm husbandry. This farmer has more tools and\n\n1\n\nmay even possess a hand tractor or hand thresher.\nt\t\n\nMany gland farmers engage in off-farm work like rattan gathering,\nabaca stripping and copra-raking on lowland farms.\n\ni\nPaddy rice farmers of the, lowland areas grow at least one crop of\nrice a year. Few farmers grow only rice and raise other crops in rotation\nor along with rice. One in three farmers on irrigated paddy cultivates\ntwo rice crops. Farmers on irrigated land generally eat 20\xe2\x80\xa2 of their rice\ncrop, and use 40% to pay for hired labor. Many paddy farmers never receive\nthe full support price of rice because of their inability to adequately\ndry or clean it. (Twenty percent of palay is hand-milled on the farm by\npounding with mortar and pestle.) Most farm units include small livestock\nand permanent fruit trees.\t\n\ns\n\nAn example of the shifting cultivator in the rainforest is the Hanunoo\nof Mindoro. The Hanunoo\'s cultivation includes the astonishing number of\n430 species of intercropped plants. H.C. Conklin, in an FAO report, wrote\nof their farming thusly:\n"At the sides and against the swidden fences there is found\nan association dominated by low-climbing or sprawling legumes\n(asparagus beans, sieva beans, hyacinth beans, string beans,\nand cowpeas). As one goes out into the center of the swidden,\none passes through an association dominated by ripening grain\ncrops, but also including numerous maturing root crops, shrub\nlegumes and tree crops. Poleclimbing yam vines, heart-shaped\ntaro leaves, ground-hugging sweet potato vines, and shrublike\nmanioc stems are the only visible signs of the large store of\nstarch staples which is building up underground, while the\ngrain crops fruit a metre or so above the swidden floor before\ngiving way to the more widely-spaced and less-rapidly maturing\ntree-crops."\n\nSpecial Crop: abaca $19,900,000 export value\nLast year\'s production of abaca has been estimated at 620,000 bales\nof 125 kilos each. This is a banana-like perennial supplying a strong\nresilient fiber commonly ;mown as Manila hemp. Shading and weeding is\n\nt\ni\n\ni\n\nnecessary for this crop (which is both wild and cultivated) until it\nt\nL\n\nreaches saturation. Then the stalk is cut and the sheaths are cut into\nstrips to get out the long fiber inside. The fiber strips are then\ndrawn by hand between\n\nthe edge of a knife blade and a wooden plane. as\n\neach drawing maket, the fiber cleaner and finer, producing a high grade\nhemp is largely a matter of hand labor. (Several machines are now in use,\n\nF t.\n\nthough quality is sometimes sacrificed.) Then-the fiber is sun-dried and\nloosely packed into bundles.\n\nt\nk\n\ni\n\nF-12\n\nLot.\n\n^-\t\n\nPHILIMMS\n\nAgricu l tural development needs\nas .rt:s! ify at= .......................\nrel\n\n^oR si_ble ply applications\n\nPalay almost entirely solar dried, \t\nwith high losses due to unequal\ntemperature and humidity causing\ncracking and over-drying\n\ndriers\n\nlittle bulk storage of rice\t\nbecause it requires strict\nmoisture control\n\nventilating fans\n\nfew mechanical driers for maize,\t\nmaize spread on concrete floors\nfor solar drying\n\ndriers\n\nvery few mechanical maize \t\nshellers exist; large increases\nof maize production is expected\nin Mindanao and Cagayan Valley\n\nshellers, mills, driers\n\nCopra kilns are used very\t\ninfrequently; tapahans often\ndry copra unequally and scorch it\n\ncopra driers\n\nmost people won\'t eat fish that \t\nis not fresh; inadequate refrigeration\n\nrefrigeration\n\n$1 million in AID fish culture \t\nproject\t\n\npumps, aerators for fish\npond aquaculture\n\nF-13\n\nSOS\nAID, 1979 "Agricultural Sector Paper."\nAID, March, 1980 conversation with Philippines desk officer, Mr. Nusbaum.\nAsian Development Bank, Asian Agricultural Survey, 19696\nBrandt, Andres, Fish Catching Methods of the World, Fishing Books Ltd.,\nLondon, 1972.\nBurley, T.M., "Mechanization of Asian Agriculture: Its Potentials and\nPitfalls, World Crops (26/2/63).\nForeign Agricultural Service, USDA, Feb. 1980 "Attache Report."\nInternational Association of Agricultural Economists, World Atlas\nof Agriculture, Instituto Geographico de Agostini, Novara, 1976.\nKurian, G.T.
